PRINCESS HATZFELDT PREPARES TO CONTEST
WILL OF COLLIS P. HUNTINGTON, AND VISITS
COAST TO GATHER EVIDENCE OF ADOPTION
THE Princess Clara von Hatzfeldt
and her titled husband arrived
most unexpectedly Sunday, and
The Call made exclusive an
nouncement of their coming and
the mystery which surrounded their
movements. The Call is able now to an
nounce that the purpose of this visit of
the foster-daughter of Collis P. Hunting
ton and the Prince von Hatzfeldt is noth
ing less than to gather evidence upon
which to base a contest of the will of the
railway magnate.
The Princess is now in Sacramento with
her mother, Mrs. Prentice, and the Prince
has made various explanations of her ab
sence, to some inquirers saying she had
gone to Burllngame and to others that
she had gone driving. The Prince refuses
to discuss the proposed will contest and
says the news will be given out to all the
papers in due time.
The purpose of the visit of the Princess
von Hatzfeldt to Sacramento is to pro
cure if possible documentary evidence of
her adoption by Collis P. Huntington. and
if documentary evidence is not procurable
Bhe will seek cumulative evidence that
Huntington regarded her as his adopted
daughter and let It be generally under
stood that he had legally adopted her.
Arrivals Many and Mysterious.
Joseph Redditg, attorney for the Von
Hatzfeldts, came out from New York
early last week and established himself
at the Bohemian Club. His coming also
was unheralded, and his visit vas appar
ently without serious or important pur
pose. But when^his clients reached Cal
ifornia he knew of their coming and went
"up the road" to meet them and accom
panied them back to San Francisco. Yes
terday he was in frequent consultation
with the Prince and the two were in com-
The Prince's eagerness to keep secret
the fact that the Princess was in Sacra
mento is further shown by his statements
to other inquirers that she had merely
gone out driving and would be in later in
the day. The Prince also stated that their
stay in San Francisco would be very
short, only a few days, in fact,, and that
their return would be via the Canadian
route. In spite of this the apartments at
the Palace are engaged for an indefinite
time and "their stay will be gauged by the
slipped into San Francisco and went to
apartments at th\ Palace Hotel and none
knew that the great hotel sheltered them.
Yesterday morning, as the result of The
Call's exclusive announcement of their
presence, many cards were sent up and
representatives of all the newspapers
sought Interviews with both the Princess
and her titled husband. Tlie Prince him
self granted interviews and explained that
his wife had gone to San Mateo for a few
days' rest. He and the Princess had come
to California, he said,* merely for a short
pleasure trip. His reticence and his fre
quent and protracted consultations with
his attorney did not serve to lend force
to his explanation of the purpose of the
visit to the land of his wife's hjrth.
MRS. C. P. HUNTINGTON.
r'TT 5^ HE leaders of the charity workers
I and some of their assistants are
IS in jail charged with conspiracy

W. "W. Doneghy. Helen Barnes, Julia
Howie, Belle Smith, Isabelle McDonald
and Mrs. C. E. "Wilcox were arrested last
evening by Detectives Wren and Dinan
on warrants issued on the complaint of
Charles A. Smith, assistant cashier of the
Columbian Banking Company, who last
week purchased some of the benefit tick
ets of the bogus* "California Boys' and
Girls* Aid Society."
Harry Mamlock, the sleek operator,
who was one of the prime movers In de
frauding the public on the fake concert
scheme, disappeared from the city on
Sunday night. The police searched the
tenderloin for him last night, but without
success. Mamlock did not even take any
of his clothing or effects with him, but
left all his belongings in the room he oc
cupied In a boarding-house at the corner
of Post and Leavenworth streets.
. The reluctance of some of the dupes of
the band of "charity workers" to come
forward and swear to a complaint opened
a way for MamloA to slip through the
fingers of the police. -The swindler was
detained In custody at the Hall of Jus
tice for two hours on Sunday, but Cap
tain Seymour set him at liberty, as It was
impossible to secure a complaint charg
ing fraud.
Cashier Smith Swears to Complaint.
Yesterday afternoon Cashier Smith of
the Columbian Banking Company was
willing to do his duty as a citizen, and by
swearing to a complaint set in motion the
machinery of the law calculated to ef
fectually end the work of the charity
fakers and swindlers and at the same
time protect the charitable public.
After the complaint of Cashier Smith
had been sworn to before Police Judge
A. J. Fritz the - warra nts were signed by
his Honor, and Assistant District Attor
neys Perry and Burke soon had the doc
uments in proper order. Eight warrants
were issued by Judge Fritz.
As soon as the warrants had been hand
ed over to Chief of Detectives Seymour
that official set his force at" work to bring
In the accused parties.
Detectives "Wren and Dlnan proceeded
to room 612, Emma Spreckels building,
where Doneghy, Helen Barnes and the
rest of his - outfit have been located for the
last three weeks. Entering the room the
detectives found Doneghy, Miss Barnes
and a few other persons there. The of
ficers of the law wasted no time, but
quickly placed Doneghy and the woman
under arrest. The warrants were read to
them, and when Helen Barnes desired to
know who Charles A- Smith was, who
had sworn to the complaints, the detec
tives replied that they did not know,
which was the truth.
The arrested persons were taken to the
Hall of Justice and kept in the detectives*
room until the rest of those wanted could
be found. At the boarding-house on Post
and Leavenworth streets the detectives
located Julie Howie, Belle Smith and Mrs.
C. E. "Wilcox. The women took their ar
rest with coolness and only asked for a
chance to "fix their hair."
Mamlock Makes His Escape. *
The room in the house which Mamlock
occupied, adjoining that of his three wo
men accomplices, was then visited by tha
detectives, but Mamlock was missing.
Inquiries made of the three arrested
women led to answers that they had not
seen Mamlock since Sunday evening at
dinner, and the servants in the house In
formed the police that his room had not
been occupied on Sunday night.
The second batch of arrested charity
schemers was taken to police headquar
ters, â¢ and, with Doneghy and Helen
Barnes, were booked on the charge of
conspiracy to obtain money by false pre
tenses.
Doneghy gave his occupation as a "con
cert promoter," residence New York;
Helen Barnes was booked as of "no oc
cupation." residence New York; Julie
Howie stated that she was a milliner of
Los Angeles; Mrs. C. E. "Wllcox was book
ed as a "trained nurse," also of Los An
geles, and Belle Smith gave her residence
as Santa Barbara.
. The police were unable to locate Miss
Detweiler, one of the schemes, who came
from the East with Doneghy. Isabelle"*"
McDonald was arrested late at night by
Detective Hamill and locked up.
Judge Fritz, ift issuing the warrants,
was so impressed with the seriousness of
the offense charged that he fixed the bail
in each case at $1000 bonds or 1300 cash.
None of the schemers were able last night
to secure bail and they spent the % night
behind prison bars.

THE members of the House Com
mittee on Rivers and Harbors who
have been In California for several
days examining into the needs of
\ the waterways of the State are ex
i .ttefl to arrive in San Francisco this af
ternoon. Their mission is of vast Impor
tance to California, and every effort will
be made by the public-spirited citizens of
this city, as well as of other cities in the
State, to fa.cIUU.te the gathering: of infor
mation by the Congressional investigators
that will enable them to formulate a. com
prehensive report on the subject that will.
It is hoped, result in an adequate appropri
ation by the next Congress for this impor
tant work.
Members of California's Congressional
delegation have in interviews given clean
cut and logical expositions of the condi
tions that now exist and the necessity of
Congressional aid in the development of
the wonderful resources of the State they
represent. Their statements are support
ed by statistics, and they stand ready to
point out and explain facts to the mem
bers of the committee that can hardly fail
to impress them that the commercial
greatness of this city and State will be
gTeatly enhanced by favorable Congres
sional action for the betterment of Call>
f ornia's rivers and harbors. *
Senator George C. Perkins, Congressmen
Mctcalf, Woods, Kahn and Coombs give
valuable Information on the subject, and
Irving M. Scott takes a broad view of the
scope that Intelligent harbor improvement
may take, dealing with matters within
end beyond the authority of Congress.
To Be Received by Business Men.
The members of the committee will be
received on their arrival here by repre
tentatlves of the commercial bodies of
this city, including such men as William
2X. Bunker of the Chamber of Commerce;
Thomas J. Parsons of the Board of Trade:
A. Sbarboro of the Manufacturers* and
Producers' Association: Moses Greene
baura. of the Merchants' Association: N.
P. Chiprnan of the California State'Board
of Trade, and George E. Plummer of the
committee on commerce.
The Congressmen will first visit Oakland
and consider the needs of that city. Next
they will view the bay of San Francisco
and will visit Vallejo and Napa. Then
they â¢will g-o to Stockton. Everywhere
they will receive a California welcome,
which was presaged by the imitations
eent out by the California Legislature
and by the commercial bodies. A pro
gramme of entertainment has been pre
pared by the commercial committee, of
which William M. Eunker 1b the chair
man. In the various cities to be visited
local committees have arranged to make
the visit of the Congressmen pleasant and
Instructive.
The members of the Congressional com
mittee are accompanied by members of
their families and friends. The party
includes:
Theodore E. Burton, chairman of the com
mittee; Mr. and Mrs. Walter Reeves. Roswell
P. Bishop and eon, Mr. and lire. Ernest F.
Acheson. Mr. and Mrs. Page Morris, Mr. and

tfce committee; Frank H. Hitchcock, chief of

ment of Agriculture; Mr. Henche, stenographer,
end Mrs. Henche; Mr. Crist, messenger.
The visit of the Congressmen is recog
nized in all quarters as being Important
and significant. The distinguished mem
bers of the House of Representatives are
traveling thousands of miles In search of
information, which will be gladly given.
They come to the Pacific Coast to see
personally the rivers and harbors and to
consider the needs of the people which
can be met by Congress. Their journey
Is unique In American history. As Con
gressman Metcalf says In an interview:
In the history of the American
Congress it is the first time that a
great committee has traveled thou
sands of miles to personally inspect
and inquire into the needs and possi-
tilities of our State in the all-im
portant matter of our rivers and har
bor improvements. They are here by
the special invitation of the-Legisla
ture of this State and the various
commercial bodies of California, pre
sented by the united California dele
gation in both Houses of Congress,
end they come without any appropri-
ft tion by Congress to meet -the ex-
pense of the trip.
The Fifty-sixth Congress failed to pass
a river and harbor bill owing to well un
derstood causes. The question of the util
ization cf rivers that are not navigable
for the purpose of irrigating arid lands
was coupled with the improvement of
navigation, and the bill failed. California
was very largely interested. The facts
are bo well understood in the several lo-
ragresslonal Investigators. Are Ex
pected To-I>ay and Will Be Wel

Prince Not * Ready . to Talk.
Prince Hatzfeldt spent a very busy time
yesterday. After seeing his wife, the
Princess, off on the 10 o'clock train for
Sacramento, he visited the Crocker build
ing and had a lengthy conference with
Joseph Redding,- his attorney. After
lunch he paid another visit to Redding's
office, accompanied by his friend, Mons.
F.M. Guedalla, who is visiting the coast
in the Prince's party. He then went back
to the Palace Hotel and ordered a vic
toria to take him for a drive .through
Golden Gate Park. While waiting the ar
rival of the vehicle the Prince was inter
viewed by a Call, reporter.
"The Printass has gone out of town
alone, has she not?" was asked^
"Yes. She will be away for a day or
so," the Prince replied.
"She went to Sacramento, did she not?"
"Where she went I would prefer not to
say,", he replied.' ' . -
The Prince was showing signs of un
easiness.
, "Is it not a fact that the Princess is
going to contest the will of the late C.
P. Huntington?" '
. "I have nothing to say at all about the
matter at present," he answered. "When
the proper time comes I will send" for all
the newspaper men and state what I
want to tell them. I want to treat you
all alike, but I i am not ready to talk
yet."
The. vehicle was waiting and the Prince
entered the carriage, accompanied by his
friend Guedalla, * the latter adjusting his
monocle before giving the driver the
word to start.

Congressman Julius Kahn Describes
SAN FRANCISCO HARBOR.
"I trust that the committee who are now
on the Pacific Coast, after seeing â¢ the
varied and vast resources of California,
her diversified. Interests, and especially
the commerce of our ports and the great
natural waterways in the Sacramento and
San Joaquin valleys, which will enable
the farmers of that great empire to trans
port their grain and products to the mar
ket, will see the necessity of the Govern
ment aiding us in keeping them open to
commerce, thereby increasing the com
mercial trade of our coast ports and our
growing maritime Interests. These object
lessons, I think, will make a favorable im
pression upon the committee," and the Cal
ifornia delegation in Congress will have
their co-operation In securing needed ap-.
propriatlons from the Government for
these Improvements, which are of so much
importance to our State." , â¢ : :
"These recommendations have* been
carefully considered by the Government
Board of United States Engineers," said
the Senator. "The California delegation
was successful In embodying the most of
them in our bill for the improvement of
rivers and harbors, which failed to be
come a law at the last session of Con
gress. I think there is no question
that: the- present Congress will pass- an
equitable river and harbor appropriation
bill. In this bill California will ask for
an appropriation for the removal of the
sunken rocks in the harbor of San Fran
cisco, the imsrovement of the Oakland
harbor. Alviso and Petaluma channels,
Napa River, dredging at Mare Island and
Point Pinole, improvements in the San
Joaquin, Sacramento and Feather rivers;
also the port of San Luis Obispo and the
San Pedro and San Dlegro harbors, and
also dredging in Humboldt Bay.
Senator Perkins says that he could not
give a better Idea of his views in relation
to the needs of the State at large in the
matter of the improvement of Its rivers
and harbors than can be found in the
facts embraced in the report of the Gov
ernment's engineers. The California dele
gation asked Congress to make all the
appropriations that were recommended by
LJeutenant Colonel Heuer.

The search of the Sacramento County
records to which reference is- made oc
curred early In September, shortly after
Huntington's death. Men close up In the
Southern Pacific had first quietly conduct
ed incuiries and then an agent came up
from San Francisco to institute a search
It is recalled now that this man did not
state his name, nor would he state his
object further than that he was looking

â¢whose name he declined to mention. Even
the deputies â¢ in the County Clerk's and

Clara Prentice by the railroad magnate
was discovered.
Mysterious Searcher.
The opinions of. various persons were
obtained, and some of these, were to the
effect that no formal adoption, as now
provided for by law, had been made, and
that therefore there could be no such doc
ument on file. Others, however, asserted
that such a document was In existence
and that it might be found had not the
imperfect system of Indexing In vogue by
county officials at that time made the
search difficult. Mrs. Clara Prentice, the
mother of the Princess, declared that she
signed papers relinquishing her claim to
her daughter end that the. adoption was
properly put on record. She expressed the
opinion, when Interviewed at the time,
that the record was In New York, as the
adoption did not take place until Mr.
Huntington and his first wife, who was a
sister of Mrs. Prentice, went to New York
to live. !
The presence of the Princess in Sacra
mento revives interest In the fact that
shortly after the death of C. P. Hunting
ton a search was made through the musty
archives of the county by some one repre
senting the Huntington interests for a
record of the formal adoption of the
Princess by Huntington, but though the
search was vigorously carried on, the doc
ument could not be found.

San Francisco's Harbor Should Be Cleared of All Obstructions t() 'Navigation.
Oakland, Port Costa, Vallejo " and Stockton in Pressing ; NeedâGreat
Importance of More Sea Room at Mare Island Is Strongly Set Forth
"Oakland harbor already has the largest
yards for the building of wooden ships
on San Francisco Bay, and these ship
yards are turning out yearly an aggregate
tonnage larger than all other shipyards
on the shores of San Francisco Bay com
bined. The largest sailing vessels ever
built on the shores of San Francisco Bay
have been . launched from Oakland ship
yards. The coal bunkers in Oakland har
bor are among. the largest in the State.
"Along the water front of Oakland be
sides the shipyards are numerous and
large factories.. comprising cotton and jute
mills, foundries, machine, shops, planing
mills, flour mills, sash and door factories,
railroad shops, numerous large lumber
yards and a variety of other Industries.
With the further improvement of the har
bor all these will increase in number and
importance." , . -
"Moreover, during the years since- the
adoption of these plans a great change
has taken place in the size and construc
tion of ships; they are larger and draw
more water, and thus the Improvements
then contemplated are quite Inadequate to
present conditions, to say nothing of 'those
which will soon exist from the tre
mendous increase of Pacific commerce.
Oakland harbor offers possibilities for one
of the best harbors m the world, and I
am confident that to be convinced of this
the committee will need but- to inspect It.
It contains no rocks or any obstruction of
any kind which cannot be readily removed
by ordinary dredging. It is an Ideal har
bor for ship-building, repairing and dry
docks. Its entrance is only six miles from
the open sea. , â¢â¢
"The Importance of the improvement of
Oakland harbor, Its widening-, deepening
and extending, â¢ has already been recog
nized by the general Government, and
large sums of money have been appro
priated and expended thereon. As far
back as 1873 a beard of officers was con
stituted to make the examination, survey
and plans for Oakland harbor, In accord
ance with the provisions of an act of
Congress approved March 3, 1873. The
plans prepared were adopted in 1874, and
the work of improvement was placed In
charge of Colonel Mendell. Congress has
continued to make appropriations from
time to time since then aggregating $2,
450,000. It must not be forgotten, how
ever," that since the adoption of plans for
this harbor improvement, twenty-seven
years ago, the commerce of Oakland has
grown to a magnitude which could not at
that time, have been imagined by the
most sanguine. In 1874 the freight ton
nage amounted to 154,300 tons; in 1900 it
amounted to 3,263,212 tons, a twenty-fold
increase.
Harbor of Oakland.
"I would also call attention to the great
benefit to bÂ« derived from deepening the
channel between Antloch and the Straits
of Carquinez. This improvement would
not cost to exceed $25,000 and would en
able deep water vessels to land at Antioch
and open up to deep water commerce an
immensely productive and fertile region
of country.
By dredging the channel at Pinole
Point and removing Commission
Bock from the Mare Island Channel;
the largest battleship could be taken
to the yard for repairs r^and this 'would
result in hundreds of thousands of
dollars being paidV.but. annually in
this State by the Government for ad
ditional labor and material. At the
present time all' the battleships are
taken to the Bremerton Station at
Puget Sound to be docked and re
paired, but with the improvements
above referred to they could, and
would, be all repaired at Mare
Island Navy Yard.
Herbert .W. Bowen, who becomes the
new Minister to Venezuela, has a most
creditable -Â¦Â¦ record in the consular and
diplomatic service. He became United
States Consul at Barcelona in 1890, and
his office at that post was raised to the
rank of Consul General five years af
terward. Mr. Bowen stuck to his post
through the exciting days preceding the
Spanish-American war as long as any
American could have found safety on
Spanish soil. At the conclusion of the
war he was about to return to his old
post, but instead was appointed Minister
Resident and Consul General to Persia.

DESPERATE INDIAiN
SHOT BY PURSUERS
The theory of murder is further cor
roborated by the fact 'that three young
TACOMA, June 17.â One of the most
atrocious murders ever committed in this
State is reported to-night from North
Yakima. This morning the body of
Charles J. Kampeter, a. popular young
man of North Yakima, aged 22 years,
was found on the Northern Pacific track
a mile north of town. Both legs were
cut off and the body, was badly mangled,
i The North Coast limited passenger
train had passed through three nours be
fore, causing the supposition that Kam
peter had in some manner been struck by
the train and killed. Closer investiga
tion by the authorities and relatives
proved beyond doubt that he was mur
dered by desperadoes, of whom several
have been operating in and around North
Yakima for several days. It was found
that Kampeter's watch and money had
been taken. His hat wa3 found by the
Eide of the water-tank, a mile away from
where the body was run over. There were
three indentations in the hat and his
head bore three plain marks of the use
of a club.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
men of North Yakima had an encounter
with bandits near the lumber yards of
the St. Paul and Tacoma Lumber Com
pany about 9 o'clock last night. Though
commanded to surrender and be relieved
of their valuables they succeeded In
breaking away and went ui> town. An
hour later they started home and were
again met by the footpads. This time
they secured reinforcements and were
then permitted to return unmolested.
Kampeter left town for hia home soon
after this occurred. His course took him
in the same direction, and no doubt exists
that he was killed while giving resistance
to the footpads who robbed him. His
body was then carried down the railroad
track and deposited where the early
morning passenger train would run over
Xorth Yakima is greatly aroused over
the murder and is forming a vigilance
committee to rid the town of all sus
picious characters. Bloodhounds are on
the trail of Kampeter's murderers. The
murdered man is a son of Henry Kam
peter, a well-known gardener. He had
considerable money In his possession and
had Intended leaving to-day for a trip to
Tacoma. -

Special Dispatch to The Call.
TACOMA, Wash., June 17. â The fishing
schooner White Wings la reported to
have been wrecked between, the north end
of Vancouver Island and the south end of
Moresby Island. Thirty lives were lost,
according to the report.
Four days ago the schooner was three
weeks overdue at Petersburg, in Wrangel
Narrows, where she has been, employed
catching halibut by the Icy Strait Pack
ing Company, which owns her. The White
Wings left Seattle last month for Peters
burg with a crew of sixteen men. She

Strikers' Demands- Are Met.
CHICAGO, June 17.â Settlements with
striking machinists were effected to-day
at five plants of local manufacturers,
making a total of over seventy Chicago
plants in which the strikers have won the
fight begun through the general strike of
three weeks ago.
Newport News Company Lays Off
Many of Its Men.
NEWPORT NEWS, ' Va., June 17.â
Twelve hundred men from the various de
partments were laid off at the plant of
the Newport News Shipbuilding and Dry
dock Company to-day, and as many more
will be laid off -to-morrow if the striking
machinists do not return to work. The
management will retain only enough men
to handle repair work, notably on the
North German I^loyd liner Maine, which
is being rebuilt. The departments chiefly
affected to-day are those of the boiler
makers, blacksmiths, carpenters and join
ers and shipfltters.
HUNBKEDS LOSE POSITIONS.
SEATTLE, June 17.â Moran Brothers' ]
shipyard, foundry and lumber mill plant <
In this city Is destined to be the storm,
center of the machinists' strike, which ]
has paralyzed certain Industries from one ,
end of the coast to the other for several
weeks. ,
Armed guards are on duty at the big
plant and work was resumed to-day with
non-union men on the steamship Garonne.
When the strikers discovered the pres
ence of men with rifles inside the big
gates the word was immediately passed
along the line. Whatever may be the .
plans of the strikers." to meet the new :
condition of affairs they asserted at their .
headquarters. this afternoon that no vio
lence was intended. Â¦ â¢ ]
Matters were more complicated, at 3:15 '
o'clock this afternoon by a mysterious \
tire, discovered in a pile of garbage on Â¦
the wnarf at Moran's. The nre depart- :
ment made a quick run and extinguished 'Â¦
tae biaze before it had done damage of ]
consequence. This incident has fanned 'Â¦
gossip, and, despite the fact that no
statements have Deen given out to lay ,
ti.e blame of the nre in any particular
direction, considerable feeling has been Â¦
aroused. ,
Non-Union Men at Work.
Up to this time the differences of opin
ion between the striking machinists and
the company has been argued in a peace
able manner. It appears, however, that
the Morans have secured non-union men
from Portland and San Francisco to take
the places of the strikers. They started
up 10-day with fully seventy-rive car
penters and sixty machinists. William
Moran of the company to-day stated that
more non-union mechanics would" be put I
to work every day trom now on and tnat
in a short time it was expected that the
big plant would be running as usual. The
non-union mechanics are working ten
hours a day at the old scale of wages,
which averages ?3 50 a day for workmen
in the metal-working line.
At the company's offices it was stated
that applications for employment from
non-union machinists are being received
dally and that there would be no diffi
culty in obtaining a complete working
force in time. The non-union men come
from San Francisco, 1'ortland and from
various points in the East. So quietly
were the oreparations for the resumption
of work conducted that very few people
knew to-day that a non-union crew had
been put to work. No statement could be
obtained regarding the attitude of the
strikers' committee save that the com
mittee would not attempt to interfere
with the men.
Work in the machine, bollermaklng and
molding departments of every large metal
working plant in the city has been sus
pended plnce May 20, the date when the
last of the union mechanics in the metal
â¢trades went out." About * 1200 men quit
their benches in this city.
v Plant Strongly Guarded.
None of the members of the Metal
Workers' Association will say how many
men have been secured"to take the places
made vacant by the strikers. It is
known, however, that a considerable
number of men have signed to go to work
for the Morans, and it is said that other
local concerns are following their ex
emple. While tho statement is made that
every effort will be put forth to prevent
trouble, yet the fact that the Moran Com
'pany is preparing, with the aid of armed
"men, to protect its employes and the
plant is held to be evidence that the of
ficers of the company* have reasons to
fear interference if not actual evidence
from the striking v/orkmen.
There are about twenty guards on duty
at the Moran plant at present, but the
services of a score or more men have
been secured and they are ready, it is
stated, to go to work at the first intima
tion of danger. E. A. Gardner, a well
known detective, is in charge of the
guards. His men are experienced, and he
says will be prepared for all emergencies.
The Morans to-day resumed work on
the Garonne, formerly an army transport,
which has been tied up for some weeks
because of the machinists' strike. Will
iam Moran said that the ship would be
readv for commission July 1. The gates
at the big plant are closed and closely
gOarded. It is as difficult to gain en
trance as it is to a fort in time of war.
The Morans' action leaves no room for
doubt that the big fight that will settle
the fate of the strike, which is being felt
all over the Pacific Coast, has at last
found its center. To-night everything is
quiet and no demand has been made 'on
the police or the Sheriff for action.

' SAN RAFAEL, June 17.â This morning
Superior Judge Angellotti sentenced W.
F. Warburton to thirty yeafs' imprison
ment in San Quentln prison. Warburton
was found guilty by a jury of murder in
the second degree for the shooting of
Matthew ReiUy at Billy Shannon's resort
in this city last January. Attorneys Len
non and Hawkins to-day moved for a new
trial upon technical grounds, and \Jater
when this was denied "made a plea for
clemency for the defendant. In passing
sentence Judge Angellotti reviewed the
testimony given at the trial and stated
that he considered the verdict a just one.
Thirty Years for Warburton.
After viewing the harbor the commit
tee were driven to the Big Trees by W.
H. Bias, M. Besse and J. F. Coope.
the following reception committee: D. C.
Clark, president of the Board of Trade;
Mayor Parker, W. T. Jeter, F. A. Hlhn,
F. W. Billing. 0. J. Lincoln, Postmaster;
H. F. Kron. E. TTrGarrett, A. A. Taylor
and D. McPherson.
This* morning the First and Second
Brigades were out over the hills for bat
talion drill. There was three hours of it
in open order, line formation and then
marchings. The drill was from 8 o'clock
to 11. and no time was lost. The four
troops of cavalry went down to the beach,
drilling there and on the way back and
forth, while the signal corps spent the
morning coiling wire and arranging equip
ape for the detail that marched away
with the Third Brigade.
The signal corps has done an immense
amount of work in the camp since it
opened. In every brigade headquarters
and in the division headquarters tele
phones have been put up, and the sys
tem is being constantly extended. The
combined corps of the three brigades are
EKgades on the Hills.
to take any messages that might be sent
back by flags or lamps or otherwise.
To-morrow morning the brigade will re
turn and its first duty will be to make
a full report of the march, including a
map of the country passed through, and a
detailed description of it. To-morrow,
also, the First Brigade will make a simi
lar march under similar circumstances
and under similar orders. They will take
Batteries A and D of the Artillery Batta
lion with the Naval Militia guns in addi
tion to the cavalry and signal corps.
The cavalry escort for the First Brigade
will be Troops A and D. Batteries A. and
D were detailed to accompany the Third
Brigade to-day, but the Naval Militia
guns had not been delivered by the rail
road and so the order was countermand
ed. The guns of the Naval Militia were
loaded, on the' cars at the Arctic Oil
Works wharf in San Francisco, where the
Naval Militia ship Marion lies, last Fri
day, and since then the car has remained
there. It was only after General Dickin
son demanded that the grins be delivered
at once lhat the car was looked for and
found. Tt was still at the oil wharf. The
guns will be in to-morrow.

NEWS OF THE OCEAN".
The big freight steamer Hawaiian ar
rived from Honolulu yesterday with 8600
tons of sugar. This Is the largest cargo
ever brought from the islands. The ves
sel -was damaged while among the islands
and came here with her cargo instead of
going to New York. After discharging she
will eo into the drydock. Â»
T. J. Flynn, while unloading a barrel of
whisky, met with, a serious accident yes
terday. The barrel slipped and fell on
his right leg:, breaking It.
The Licensed Baggage Solicitors* Union
No. 1 will Tglve a smoker In its rooms at
102 O'Farrell street to-morrow night. AH
the solicitors for the hotels and the trans
fer men on the front have been invited to
the affair. A cood time is expected.
The Shipwrights' Association presented
John Kentfield, of Kentfleld & Co., ship
owners, with a very handsome testimonial
yesterday. Mr. Kentfield gave the asso
ciation a beautiful American flag and the
testimonial was not only a letter of
thanks, but an acknowledgment of. the
kindly feeling existing between the ship
owner and the association.

panies will be landed at the Presidio to

On June 8, in latitude 43 degrees 11 min
utes north, longitude 166 degrees 28 min
utes west, the transport Buford was
spoken. The Buford left Nagasaki on
May 26 and was expected here last Sun
jay. Her captain reported that his supply
of. coal was of the poorest and that he
was going to Honolulu for a new lot. The
buford was then 1382 miles from Honolulu
and 2000 from San Francisco. Captain
Merkens said he expected to make Hono
lulu by June 14 and would leave the next
&ay, arriving in San Francisco on June 24.
The Pennsylvania brings up the head
quarters and band and twelve companies
of the Fortieth, numbering 909 men all
told, ten members of the hospital corps,
twelve prisoners, five officers' servants,
five stowaways and ninety-eight- dis
charged men. Those who came home In

The United States army transport Penn
sylvania arrived from Manila direct yes
terday afternoon. She brings home the
Fortieth Infantry and a number of dis
charged men.
Back From the Philippines.
The Philadelphia is to sail to-day for
Samoa. She has yet to take aboard four
anchors and fifty tons of chain, and
should this not arrive it will be next week
before the cruiser gets away. Forty men
to complete the Philadelphia's crew came
down from Mare Island yesterday.

The battleship Iowa sails for Puget
Sound to-morrow. During her absence
she will engage in target practice off
Port Angeles.

Nearly 300 tons of munitions of war have
to be removed from the battleship during;
the week, and yesterday a barge from
the navy yard was alongside taking the
stuff aboard. After it is discharged t ae
Oregon will probably go to the Bremerton
navy yard.

- At l:50Â«p. m. the men were sent away
to a fair start. Both crews were a little
unsteady at first, the Iowas rowing SS
strokes to the minute to the Wisconsins
4L For the first two miles there was not
half a boat's length between the contest
ants, the ' Wisconsins having the best of
it. The Iowas Increased- their stroke to
49 'and the Wisconsins responded with 44
to the minute. The latter boat then be
gan to draw away and when three miles
had been covered the Wisconsin was
eipht boat-lengths ahead. The Iowa was
then rowing 3S and the- Wisconsin 42
strokes to the minute.
Just about this time the stroke of the
Iowa caught a orab. and a few minutes
later No. 2 on the port bow accidentally
unshipped his oar. This gave the Wiscon
sin a lead of fifteen boat-lengths, which
the Iowa boys were never able to close.
As the winning boat passed the Wiscon
sin the boys on the battleship ran half a
dozen brooms to the top of the mast
and cheered themselves hoarse, while the
band on the Iowa played "See the Con
quering Heroes Come." The official time
for the race Â¦was 37 minutes 15 seconds â
very fast considering the circumstances.
About noon yesterday Lieutenant
O'Neill of the Wisconsin went aboard the
Iowa and in a few minutes all the pre
liminaries Â¦were arranged. At 1 p. m. the
crews got into the ships' launches, the
racing- barges were made fast behind and
a start was made for Hunters Point. The
course was from a point off the dryclock
to an imaginary line drawn from Folsom
street wharf to Goat Island, a distance
of four nautical miles, or four and five
eighths statute miles. The Wisconsin
boat drew the inshore position, but it
was of little assistance to her. A strong
breeze was blowing against the rowers,
which the first o'f the ebb tide was not
sufficient to neutralize.
The barges were manned as follows:
Wisconsin â F. Garvey, cockswain: C. Drake,
stroke: C. Nygard, C. Mad&on. C. Murphy. J.
R. Spratley. W. R. tThl. T. Nyland, A. Jorgen
een, C. Neubert, J. Eglit, H. T. Johnson and
C. Johnson.
Iowaâ J. Carroll, cockswain: E. Raws, stroke;
J. Tulloch, R. Bowers. W, Junior, H. Nilsson,
E. Flnnerty. A. Campbell. F. Pitsula, P. Geig
er. J. Capell. J. Beaudoin and E. H. Olsen.
t
match for $10 an oar was made. The race
was set for the 13th inst., but owing to
the Wisconsin having to go to Puget
Sound the event had to be postponed.
Then it was set for last Saturday, but
again the Wisconsin had to go to sea.
When she returned Sunday it was de
cided to hold the race yesterday at slack
water.
In the meantime the men had been
wagering freely on the event. Both sides
were confident, but in the case of the
Wisconsin the men had nearly a -whole
month's pay, behind them, while the. Iowa
boys had theirs nearly spent. Still they
managed to scrape together $1800, and the
Wisconsin men promptly covered it and
offered to bet $1500 more. The officers all
had bets among themselves, and it is
safe to say that fully $5000 changed hands
on the result. The outcome puts a new
champion ere* 1 in the field and the Iowa
boys will in future have to take a back
seat.

THE much talked about race be
tween a picked crew from the
flagship Iowa and one from the
battleship Wisconsin came off yes
terday afternoon. For years the
boys on the Iowa haVÂ© met all comers
and have always proved victorious. Lat
terly they came to be considered Invin
cible, and a year ago, in order to gret up
a race, a crew from the port watch had
to compete with one picked from the star
board watch. The winners of that event
rowed In the boat used In the race just
decided.
When the Wisconsin went into com
mission six months ago some of the finest
oarsmen in the navy were drafted to her.
Then a very fine racing barge was built
for the battleship, and as soon as the
men saw it they decided to challenge the
Iowa's unbeaten crew. Nothing loth, the
Iowa boys picked up 'the gauntlet, and a

TJNITE IN HOLDING
DEDICATORY CEREMONIES
Military Attaches of French and Aus
trian "Embassies Jointly Honor
Their Hero Dead.
BERLIN, June 17.â A' remarkable cere-
mony occurred at Hacenburg, Prussia,
yesterday, when the military j attaches
to the ; French and ? Austrian ! Em
bassies \ In Berlin, together with fif
ty-five German veteran associations,
jointly celebrated the dedication- of
two monuments, - one erected by Aus
tria In honor of those Austrians who fell
in 1796, during the war. with France, and
the other to the French general, Marctau,
who was mortally wounded at Altenkir
chen, Rhenish Prussia. September 20, 17*3,
and died three days after. .
At the dedication of these monuments
the bands played the Marsellalse and the
Prussian hymn. A' French attache pro
posed three cheers for Emperor William
and an Austrian attache expressed â¢ his
confidence in the durability of the drei
bund.
. The ceremony was witnessed by large
numbers of German army and navy offi
cers on the active list and high civil and
military officials from a number of dis
tricts.
SETTLERS AT LOOKOUT
AiRE LIVING IN FEAR
Indian Scare Is Dying Out, but the
People Have Doubts About
Safety.
ALTURAS, June 17.â There is still much
anxiety felt here over the recent Indian
scare at Lookout, though no definite news
has been received from that place to-day.
The Grand Jury adjourned Saturday at
r.ion and took a recess until this morning
at 10 o'clock. All of the witnesses here
from the Lookout country departed im
mediately for their homes and returned
at 10 o'clock this morning.
A reDort reached here to-day, but has
not been corroborated, that the recent
uprising of Indians In the vicinity of
Lookout was caused by troubles among
themselves. The trouble grew out of a
quarrel which happened between two In
dians over a squaw. It is thought. that
the return of the witnesses to Lookout
may frighten the Indians to abandoning
tl-at part of the county, whatever their
purpose may have been.
POOLSELLING STOPPED.
Injunction Served on the Officers of
; >%,' Harlem Track.
CHICAGO. June 17.â No.tice of injunc
tion was served this afternoon upon the
officers of the Harlem racetrack as the
result of Judge Holden having granted a
writ against poolselling at the track. The
sport was not, however, interfered with
this afternoon. Whether, the effort to stop
racing will be successful the track own
ers do not venture to say. The movement
originated in the old . war between the
poolroom keepers,' John Condon, the pro
prietor of Harlem, and' his. partner, James
O'Leary, having antagonized other pool
room men, who have succeeded in enlist
ing the help of some prominent politi
cians. The latter have the co-operation
of the Citizens' League and all racing In
Chicago may be stopped. It is announced,
however, that there will be no interfer
ence with Saturday's Derby.

MODESTO. June 17.-Philip Campbell,
aged 15 years, was drowned near War
nerville in a deep hole In the upper creek

brought to Modesto.

BIG SURPRISE TO MUSICIAN.
Calling on a friend the other evening, I :
found him, his wife and daughter deep In
a discussion as to the relative merits of
the works of Wagner, Beethoven, Men
delssohn and Chopin. They talked fluent
ly and apparently understanding^ of
Opus this and No. that, accent, phrasing,
tempo, legato, staccato and other tech
nical points. I was considerably sur
prised, as I did not know that any of
them were musicians, and I said to my
friend: "How is this? You are the very
man whom I have often heard say that
you would not buy a handsome piano, as
you could not, and your wife and daugh
ter would not. play." My friend laughed
and passed Into the parlor, where the
piano stood, and after a few moments the
room was filled with the wonderful meJody
of a berceuse by Chopin. After the ber
ceuse came one of Brahm's Hungarian
dances. No. 5, strong and swinging, and
then came a Liszt rhapsody. By this time
I knew what had happened â my friend
had bought a Pianola.
I went into the parlor and looked at the
Pianola, as I had heard a great deal
about, it, and in ten minutes I was as en
thusiastic as my friend. It played the
most difficult classical compositions and
gave my friend, who a year before knew
no more about music than a cow, as thor
ough an appreciation of music as I my
self possess, although I- had lived in a
musical atmosphere all my life.
The result of this was that I investi
gated the instrument manufactured by
the firm- who are responsible for the Pi
anola, and I found that the claims they
were making in their advertisements fell
far short of adequately describing the ex
cellent quality of their goods.
Taking my friend as a fair sample of
the American people, it would seem that
the Pianola will educate the musical
sense of our people, cultivate, sharpen
and elevate them until we. who popularly
have been believed to be deficient in mu
sical appreciation, will become the great
est musical nation in the world.â New
Tork Press.
These wonderful instruments can be
seen and heard at Kohler & Chase'3, 30
O'Faxrell street- â¢
Tirst Experience With Pianola Makes
Him Its Firm Friend.
HAWAII'S CHIEF EXECUTIVE AND THE TERRITORIAL SECRETARY,
TO WHOM, POLITICAL ENEMIES BELIEVE, HE EXPECTS TO SUR
RENDER HIS OFFICE PERMANENTLY. A
LONDON, June 17.â Charles Lillywhite,
who claims to be a naturalized American
and who says he has been In the United
States since 1SS5, but who was arrested in
New Zealand in November last and
brought to this country yesterday,
charged with being Arthur Blath, who is
accused of murder, robbery and arson
committed at Colchester, England, in 1893,
had his first hearing to-day in camera, to
the disappointment of a number of curi
ous people who crowded the courtroom.
The discovery of relatives the prisoner
claims to have In Leeds presages a break
down of the Government case.
Arthur Blath in 1893 robbed and mur
dered Arthur Welch at Colchester. Eng
land, and set fire to his victim's tailor
shop in order to cover his crime. Blath
escaped' and no trace of him was found
until a man answering his description
Â¦was arrested at Wellington, N. Z., No
vember 16 last as the result of information
given to the police by a woman. The pris
oner was, identified as Blath at Welling
ton by two residents of London, but the
prisoner claimed to be Charles Lillywhite
and said he had an aunt named Caroline
Cook living in Chicago. He also said he
had homesteaded in the State of Wash
ington in 1892-93 and he had some docu
mentary evidence which supported his
claim.
Citizens of Tacoma are making a strong
fight to prove that Lillywhite is a victim
of mistaken identity and have forwarded
a mass of evidence to London.
American Citizen Held
Prisoner by English
Authorities.
GRAVE MISTAKE
MADE BY POLICE
Elevation of the New Or
leans Archbishop .
Probable.
WASHINGTON, June 17.â The name of
Archbishop Chapelle of New Orleans has
been brought to the attention of the Vat
ican authorities and to the Pope in person
for elevation to the rank of Cardinal be
cause of his services as Papal Delegate
for the Philippines and West Indies.
Archbishop Chapelle is of French ori
gin and it is believed that his elevation
would be looked upon with favor by !he
French authorities.
In some ecclesiastical quarters It had
been felt that the church authorities over
the Philippines, Cuba and Porto ltico
should come under the American heii
aichy, rather than under a papal dele
gate, as the American church is better
able to co-operate with the. United States
Government in directing Insular affairs.
It Is understood this question also has
been brought to the attention of the Vat
ican during the visit of Cardinal Gibbons
and , Archbishop Chapelle. â¢ During one of
the audiences, when the elevation -- of
Archbishop Chapelle was urged on the
Pope, the latter expressed his high regard
for. the work of the Archbishop. "
It is the Intention to bring the matter
of the title of the : friars in, the Philip
pines to a determination by a trial In the
Supreme Court of the Philippines. Under
tne present condition there can be no ap
peal to the Supreme Court of the United
States,- but such an appeal could be. al
lowed If provided for In legislation at the
next Congress. . f , .
LONDON, June 17.â A dispatch to the
Foreign Office from the Consul General
of Somaliland says that the Mad Mullah
expedition .had heavy fighting on May 23
The Hying column, of mounted infantry
under Captain Meriweather struck the
Mullah supply camp during a night march
and captured 5000 head of cattle, killed
one captain and captured another cov
ered 100 miles, fought a sharp action and
returned to its base, air in twenty-four
nour=.
The main force of the British, under
Colonel- Swayne. departed for Eldab on
June 2, leaving MacNeill with 300 men to
guard the zariba. Swayne's column ad
vanced against the Mullah's base. In the
meantime the Mullah with 3000 followers
attacked the zariba three times. He was
finally driven by Captain MacNeill,
with a loss of oOO men. The British in the
zariba had ten men killed and nine
wounded.
The Mullah Is now cut off from his base
and a decisive action is imminent. Â¦.
British Are Victorious in
a Series of Engage-
Sir Harry relates that the country sur
rounding Moantelgon is totally depopu
lated as a result of tribal wars and Is con
requently marvelously stocked with bis
game as tame as English park deer. Ze
bras and antelopes can be approached to
within ten yards and there is no sport in
killing them. Elephants and rhinoceroses
are also abundant, and, according, to Sir
Henry, lions in Uganda are too busy cat
ing hartebeest to notice a passing cara
van. The prehistoric giraffe has been
discovered In this country . by the Com
missioner, who proposes to maintain the
region referred to as a national park.
Sir Henry photographed a race of ape
men in the Congo forest differing entirely
from Stanley's pigmies and obtained pho
nograph records of their language and
music. He says that twelve varieties of
rubber trees are found in this country in
inexhaustible supply.

Resolved, That the clerk of this House be
instructed to communicate with the acting
Governor of the Territory and inquire of him
the nature and extent of the disability under
which the Governor of the Territory may at

In the Senate yesterday Mr. Kanuha
spoke strongly in support of a majority
report of the Military Committee in favor
of wiping out the National Guard. The
report itself w,as a roast of the extrava
gance of expenditure upon the militia. An
item among many eliciting biting sarcasm
was the payment to a fashionable tailor
of $24 for a coat for Captain A. G. Hawes
Jr., private secretary to the Governor and
one of his military aids. Mr. Kanuha
had characterized as a sheer bluff a con
tention of Mr. Achi, who is a well known
Chino-Hawaiian lawyer, that the organic
act made It obligatory upon the Territory
to maintain an efficient military estab-
lishment. Proceeding with his remarks
he used the native word "hoopunipunl,"
meaning that some one had lied.
Cecil Brown sprang up in a passion and
said that if the rest of the Senate chose
to be insulted he was to be counted out.
After the uproar Mr. Kanuha quietly fin
ished his speech.
Cecil Brown made an Impassioned
speech against the majority report. When
interrupted by Dr. Russel with a point of
order he shouted that nothing less than
throwing him out Off the window could
prevent his freedom of utterance.
Afterward the minority report was
clared the Senate adjourned.
Dr. Russcl called Mr. Kalauokalani as
Vice President to the chair. The home
rule leader complied. Dr. Russel angrily
declared that his purpose in requesting
the chair to be filled v/as to bring charges
of contempt against the Senators who had
refused to come back. In violation of the
rule empowering less than a (juorum to
summon the attendance of absent mem
bers. After some desultory talk a sug
gestion that nothing could be done in the
absence of a quorum was adopted by the
Vice President declaring the Senate ad
journed. ...

ASKS NEW TRIAL
FOR MOLINEUX
Counsel for Defendant
Severely Arraigns
Prosecution.
BUFFALO, June 17.â The Court of Ap
peals to-day heard the first argument for
a new trial for Roland B. Molineux, the
young New Yark clubman, athlete and
chemist, under sentence of death for poi
soning Mrs. Katherine J. Adams In De
cember, 1S5S. Mrs. Adams was the unin
tended victim of a plot, veiled by the use
of the American post, to take the life of
Harry Cornish, manager of athletic
sports.
Judges Parker, Vann, Bartlett, Gray,
O'Brien, Haight and Werner, in their
gowns of black, sat upon the bench to
hear the famous case, and at the counsel's
table were ex-Senator David B. Hill, who
holds the brief of the people, %and John G.
Milburn, president of the Pan-American
Exposition, who espouses the cause of the
young prisoner at Sing Sing. With Hill
fat Assistant District Attorney Le Bar
bier of New York, and grouped about
Milburn were Georg-e Gordon Battle and
Barlow S. Weeks, who defended Molineux
at his trial; General ; Leslie Molineux,
father of the prisoner, and David N. Ca.r
valho. handwriting expert, who advised
the defense. Many more than the court
room would hold crowded into the cor
ridors to hear the etory of ' the almost
fascinating crime linked with the mys
tery of the death of Henry C. Barnett.
The officers of the court barred the outer
doors and admitted the favored ones by
a rear entrance. Milburn for the appel
lant talked during- the entire session of
the court, gaining a hearing at 10:30
o'clock, when a civil case closed, and
speaking until 2 o'clock, when the court
arose for the day. He has another hour
to speak, it being agTeed that each side
should have four hours, and concludes in
the morning.
Senator Hill sat throughout the entire
speech without an interruption that
called him to nis feet.
ililburns address of three and one-half
hours' duration was directed largely to
the alleged error of the lower court in
admitting evidence as to the death of
Heny C. Barnett, and he spared neither
Recorder Goff, the trial Judge, for his rul
ings on evidence, nor the District Attor
ney's staff for its methods in securing the
admission of evidence. He spent nearly
two hours in a review of the known facts
of the two cases, and sought to show
what he described as the llimsv fabric
upon which the entire case rested.
CANNERS FLING
THE GAUNTLET
Demands of Northwest
ern Fishermen Are
Refused.
Great Strike Is Certain and
Serious Trouble Is
Feared. .
Special Dispatch to The Call.
VANCOUVER, B. C. June 17.-A11 ne
gotiations were ended and the joint meet-
Ing between canners and fishermen broke
up this afternoon finally after another at
tempt to settle the threatened strike.
The result of Saturday's meeting of the
Fishermen's Union was to hold out for
12H cents for the season. This was form
ally submitted to the canner* this after
noon and quickly declined. The canners
emphasized the trouble of the situation
by withdrawing all concessions previously i
made and declining to consider any fur- !
tner propositions. To-night representative
men on both sides look for a strike the j
moment the season opens and say that i
trouble cannot possibly be avoided. '
The Japanese working on the river had
ft . separate meetinc to-day and declared !
generally In favor of accepting the pro- j
posal of the canners. Details will be gone j
into at a later meeting. The Japanese :
were expected to stand with the white t
fishermen, at least up to the commence- '
ment of the run and their present action
was not expected. There are over SOtM >
Japanese on the river, and those, with a ]
few white men. would be * uffieient to I
supply the canneries in the event of a j
heavy season's pack. Both parties left I
to-day's* conference In high temper and I
it is unlikely that negotiations will be re- I
newed.
EARL RUSSELL
UNDER ARREST
Nobleman Who Married
in Reno Accused of
Bigamy.
Arraigned in a London Police
Court and Remanded
Â¦ on Bail.
LONDON, June 17.â Earl Russell was
arrested to-day on a : charge of having
contracted a bigamous marriage in the
United States. The Earl was met at the
railway station on his arrival f rora the
country by detectives with a warrant and
was taken to the Bow-street Police Court,
where he was formally charged. The no
bleman appeared to be quite unconcerned.
While Earl Russell awaited in the ante
room the summons to appear before 'the
magistrate the woman whom he married
in America joined him. When the case
was called a representative of the public
prosecutor said the prisoner was charged
with feloniously marrying Mrs. Mollle
Somervllle, daughter of the late George
C. Cooke of Combarnauld, Scotland.
The prosecution proceeded to outline the
Earl's marriage to Mabel Scott (his first
Countess), the separation, his subsequent
disappearance from England with a
neighbor, Mrs. Somerville, and the discov
ery that he tend Mrs. Scmerville were to
gether at Reno, New On April 14, 1900,
Earl Russell obtained n license to marry
Mollie Cooke, otherwise Mrs. Somerville,
In Nevada, and a Judge performed the
ceremony on April 15.
The prosecution announced that the Ne
vada District Judge, Mr. Koehler, and
witnesses of the marriage had arrived in
England to prove the ceremony.
Counsel for the defense intimated that
Earl Russell, having secured a divorce in
America, believed his subsequent mar
riage was legal. They pointed out that
the prosecution omitted mention of the
divorce proceedings instituted by hl3
lordship in America...
The Earl wns remanded Â«in Â£2000 ball.
In the event of a conviction the case will
be taken to the House of Lords, as Earl
Russell Is entitled to a trial by his peers.
DAMAGES DENIED
IN ITATA CASE
Vessel Was LawfullyJDe
tained, Says Claims
. Commission.
WASHINGTON, June 17.â The United
States and Chilean Claims Commission
completed its work to-day. The last case
to be decided was that involving the seiz
ure in 1S91 of the Chilean vessel Itata by
the authorities of the United States on
the ground that she was carrying a cargo
of contraband arms to Chile. The claim
was Â£44,051 with interest, and was made
by the South American Steamship . Com
pany, which had chartered the ship to
the Chilean Government. The decision
to-day dismisses the case. Minister Pioda
of Switzerland, president of the commis
sion, and Mr. Gage, the American Com
missioner, agreed in this, while Mr. Vi
cuna of Chile dissented. The commission
sums its finding as follows:
Firstâ That the i damages alleged by the
plaintiffs were -not occasioned by any unjus
tifiable action on the part of the " United
States; that the Itata was not pursued by the
naval authorities of the United States upon
the high seas into Chilean water, induced to
surrender by display of superior force and
brought back under duress. "
Second â That the Itata was voluntarlly
placed at the disposal of the United States by
the provisional government of Chile.
Thirdâ That there was probable cause for the
detention of the Itata at San Dleeo by the
authorities of the United States and there
fore no wrong was done.
Fourthâ That the claimant has brought suit
in the courts of Chile against the Govern
ment of Chile to recover damages upon the
identical claim that is here set up against
the United States; that the company has re
covered Judgment thereon and that these Judg
ments have been paid to the company by the
Government of Chile. The case must there
fore be dismissed.
Another important case decided to-day
was that of the Central and South Amer
ican Telegraph Company against Chile,
growing out of a tax of 2 cents a mes
sage levied during the uprising of 1891.
The commission awarded $4000 damages.
During the sessions of the commission
there have been seventeen cases against
Chile and two against the United States.
The total of American claims against
Chile was $3,400,000 and of this $28,062, or
.0S4 per cent, has been awarded by the
commission. The two Chilean claims
aeainst this Government were the -Itata
case, disposed of to-day, and that of
Richard Trumbuli; who was awarded
$3000 for services to the United States Le
gation in Chile in an extradition case
some years apro.

Efi ' EhThe Kansas City woman. who killed her husband
and added horror to the act by kicking the dead body
in the face is discrediting the current theories of mur
der as a fine art by showing signs of emotion at the
trial.
A Georgia negro recently applied to an attorney
to collect $10 due him by another darky, and when
asked why the debtor didn't pay, he explained: "He
says he has been owing me that money so long that
the interest has done eat up the principal, and now.
he don't' owe me anything." As a new way of pay
ing old debts that takes the cake.
American sovereignty has brought trouble as well as
a pension to the Sultan of Jolo. He has long collected
a tax from the pearl fishers in the waters around his
islands, but now foreign corporations have bought up
the fisheries and are working them with improved ap
pliances, and to the Sultan's disgust they refuse to
pay the tax. This is the first time the Sultan ever ran
up against a tax shirker, and he appears to be losing
sleep over it
Justice Harlan is a brave and learned judicial of
ficer, and has that independent spirit which is needed
on the bench, where judicial courage is of the highest
importance. It is probable that he would be the last
man to consent to invade and destroy the history and
traditions of that bench.
Among the many feather-headed and harmful
propositions for which Mr. Bryan is responsible this
is probably the worst and is conceived in the most
reckless spirit.
It is among the laws unwritten in American public
life that it is not well to tempt Judges with that great
prize as a reward for their judicial action. No one
has ever accused or suspected McLean, Chase or Field
of sophisticating their judicial action from any such
motive, but it is easy to see that when the road from
the bench to the Presidency is once opened the effect
Â¦will be that some on the bench will keep half an eye
on the law and an eye and a half on popular applause,
' to the degradation of the court.
He favors Harlan because he thinks his decisions
are popular. The people have never taken kindly to
the proposition to look on the Supreme bench for
Presidential timber. Justice McLean of Ohio was
talked of as the Republican candidate in i860, but it
was more to conciliate the old Whig element than
for any other purpose, and the proposition made no
headway against Lincoln. After the close of the
Civil War Chief Justice Chase was taken up by cer
tain elements in the Democratic party, and himself
promoted his candidacj- in 1868, but the movement did
not become popular. David Davis was looked upon
for a time as Presidential timber, but.never got near
being nominated In 18S0 and 1884 the South, in
gratitude for his decisions on the reconstruction
measures, favored the nomination of Justice Field,
and he came nearer the nomination than any other
member of that court. It is believed that if Califor
nia had given him a united support he would have
succeeded. It is not probable that the elements which
â¢were in his favor will ever again be so combined as
to come as near overcoming the popular indisposition.

At the time of the conference of the Cuban envoys

manner; in which the ,wrist is Â¦t'urne.d- at
the - time of - pitching. The .; ball i moving

twisting backward. vThe compression of
the.' air forces Â¦ the * ball gradually to the

the course being in the direction 1 in- which
the front of the ball is' twisting. r

perous to live better than ever before in recent times.
FROM a 'summary, published in ,the /London
papers of the recently issued annual .report of
the British Board of Agriculture, it appears the
past year was an exceptionally bad one for the farm
ers of 'the kingdom. It is stated that almost every
crop yielded less per acre than in the previous year.
The cultivation of wheat continues to. decline, the
area devoted' to that crop in 1900 being 156,000 acres
less_ than in 1899. The acreage of other cereals re
mained about the same as in the previous year, with
the exception that there was a sufficient increase in
the area' devoted to oats to account for; more than
half the land withdrawn from wheat.

Of course if such a scheme be designed it will not
be undertaken in the. crude way that. was adopted in
the division of Poland. Diplomacy has become more
subtle than it was. The movements on Korea and
Manchuria would be made very slowly.. By a gradual
process "Russia would have paramount influence in
the one and Japan in the other, and at last it would
come quite as a matter of course that the annexations
should take place in much the same way as the re
cent annexation of Finland as an integral part of the
Â¦Russian empire. ,
Should such an arrangement be made there would
be an interesting problem before the concert of Eu
rope. Russia has been held back from despoiling the
Turk, but could she be held back from despoiling the
Chinaman, if Japan were united- with her?
There is nothing of improbability in the story. The
ease with which rival nations can compound their
quarrels as soon as they perceive an opportunity to
despoil another nation by acting in concert is well
known. Diplomacy has understood that method of
establishing peace ever since the. days when Russia,
Austria and Prussia settled all their old feuds by the
easy plan of uniting to destroy and dismember Poland.
Consequently, since the eastward movement of Rus
sia is a menace to Japan, and since the antagonism
of Japan would be dangerous to Russia's- designs on
China, it is but natural it should have occurred to
some astute statesman and peacemaker that the best
thing to be done for the sake of avoiding war is to
divide Manchuria and Korea between them. . .
REPORTS of the mutual jealousy between Rus
sia and Japan and rumors of probable war in
the near future have been "common ever since
Japan defeated China and Russia took possession. of
Port Arthur. European diplomacy. has now another
tale to tell. It is said there are' reasons for believing
that the Mikado, and the Czar have found a basis of
conciliation and harmony, and that their Govern
ments are negotiating an agreement by which Japan
is to permit Russia to have a free hand in Manchuria
and Russia is to consent to let Japan direct and con
trol the affairs of Korea. ; .

CALIFORNIANS IN WASHINGTON
WASHINGTON, D. C, June 17.â The
To sum up . this necessarily most short
and incomplete account of British colonial
policy, we find that the two chief points
are the willingness to adapt the govern
ment to suit special cases and the en
couragement of trade by hqlding open the
customs door and granting equal trading
rights for all merchants. In the past the
former was the more important point, but
now, alas, that each administrator is tied
to the Colonial Office by the cable, much
of its value has departed. It is this mel
ancholy red tape cable which causes one
to welcome the assistance of chartered:
companies and private enterprise.
So long as Great Britain holds to free
trade and adaptation she will be success
ful and worthy of being followed.
Two Chief Elements of Success.
In the Malay archipelago Great Britain
has had to deal with many of the ele
ments of trouble with which the United
States is now face to face in the Philip
pines. .These she has successfully over
come and now has the satisfaction of
owning colonies at peace and growing: in
prosperity In the extreme.
The natives In the British colonies are
insured just laws and have the neces
saries of life provided for them at a rea
sonable rate. They are not allowed to
fight whenever the fit takes them, buc
must live orderly, well-behaved lives. In
several of the colonies Great Britain has
raised native regiments, notably for work
in the malarial African tropics. These
troops, led by white men, work splen
didly, and- afford a safety valve to the.
military ardor of races who for centuries
have regarded fighting as the amusement
most to be desired. If Great Britain were
a military power she could easily raise a
native army, large enough to enable her
to enforce her wishes on land as well as
on sea. However, she prefers to use the
natives as coolies or as tillers of the soil.
Who shall say her course is not a wise
one?
While Great Britain gives great lati
tude to the natives in her possessions, as
far 'as local affairs are concerned, she is
very particular that they shall acquire
no real ruling power, or at least no over
whelming voice in the ruling power. "When
the " native population outnumbers the
white in such great proportion as is usual
in Britsh coloniesâ non-self-governingâ it
would be suicidal to follow any other
course until at least the natives have
reached a high level of education and civ
ilization. Until such a time it Is far more
judicious and kinder to protect them from
the results which would inevitably befall
if they were permitted to mislegislate be
fore they have learned even their A B C'a.
After a cautious training In local and mu
nicipal government they may bo allowed
in time to have a voice In the actual gov
ernment. In countries where there are
several differing native races it is mani
festly impossible to allow members of ono
race to govern othersâ such a course
would directly lead to risings and disturb
ance of the peace.
Natives and the'Buling Power.
Though it may be said that companies
are useful in opening up countries, there
is no doubt that they must ever be super
seded in those countries after a time by
British protectorates. Great Britain owes
much to the fact that the open door and
equal privileges for all merchants in her
territories, wnether of. her nation or not,
encourage trade to an enormous extent.
The trade may not be British trade, fre
quently now it is American or German
trade, but the colony prospers and devel
ops steadily. In these days, as in tha
past, there can never be successful phi
lanthropic colonization, even if any nation
were to attempt it. Colonization must
proceed by trade, just as steam is neces
sary for a locomotive. The end attained
may be very high and people may then
look down with infinite scorn upon trade;
nevertheless, it is always trade which has
made colonization successful. France has
tried to colonize by planting a flag and
having troops occupy more or less miser
able Imitations of Paris, and she haa suc
ceeded in having an annual drain upon
her rational income of enormous propor
tions.
The Policy of the Open Door.
SUCCEEDS AS A COLONIZER.

state protectionâ a doubtful advantage,
entailing, as It does, a certain amount of
state supervision.
. It must be very evident even to the
most unobservant citizen that there Is a
far greater - probability of rapid develop
ment for a new country under such, a
company than can be the case under di
rect British control. There are dividends
to be earned and shareholders' meetings
to be faced. It is necessary, that the
company shall be acquainted, and inti
mately acquainted, with their trading
ground and its inhabitants. Under state
control there is apparently no such ne
cessity for accurate knowledge. Of
course, there is always the possibility
that the company will abuse its powers
and neglect its obligations in the anxiety
to earn an extra per cent In dividends.
Such action, however, always brings its
inevitable punishment In the shape of re
duced privileges, so It is never likely to
become popular among companies.
COPTRH
XTO-WHY GREAT BRITAIN
There can be laid down no hard and fast
rules or general policy to. describe the
colonizing of Great Britain. With each
new colony new methods are employed,
yet they all -have as a foundation the
main, principle of adaptationâ adaptation
of the government bo as to incorporate
air that is good and possible in the native
customs, and upon this stem to engraft
the buds of British progress and enlight
enment. To this policy England ." owes
much of her success in founding and
maintaining colonies. By colonies I mean
integral parts of the empireâ not costly
advertisements of a colonial policy or
battlefields for the training of soldiers
and the massing of fortunes by con
tractors.
The Englishman who settles In new
territory regards it ' as so much land to
be developed and made" to bring forth the
maximum amount of valuable produce.
He retains many of his customs and
habits, but once settled he has every in
tention of remaining to enjoy the fruits
of. his labors/" England is always "home"
to him, even if he never visits it again,
in the same way as it Is always "home"
to colonial-born Englishmen and Eng
lishwomen. This feeling toward England
is one of the most pleasing features of
the silken bonds which hold the empire
so firmly together. In the Englishman
there is innate the kno\Â»ledge that he is
par excellence tfe pioneer and explorer
of the world, and that knowledge lifts
him up well above the natives whom he
has to rule. . It gives to the English that
sublime confidence In their powers Â¦which
goes far to gain the confidence of the na
tive races. If you would gain the belief
of others you must first have belief in
yourself.
French, and German Colonizing.
-The Frenchman in his colonies seeks
ever to reproduce there the Paris of his
dreams; and for him there ever shines the
possibility of being able to return to that
city with a sufficient fortune to obviate
any departure from it in the future. Such
aspirations .may tend to the rapid acqui
sition of wealth, but they certainly, do
not help the development of the colony.
The Germans, on the other hand, seek
ever to introduce at once a system of
rigid military government, with a mili
tary governor and a strong garrison.
They turn the new colony as far as pos
sible into a recruiting ground. This meth
od is most successful in the thoroughness
with which it discourages German and
other merchants from establishing them
selves in German colonies.
British. Methods and Trade.
The British seek ever to encourage
trade in their new possessions and have
no desire to spend unnecessary money
upon warlike expeditions if they can ob
tain peace by reasonable incorporation of
native customs. The colonies of the
British empire are essentially business
concerns with very few exceptions. Pri
marily attracted by the opportunities of
successful trade, the British have been
forced, as that trade developed, to extend
their trading grounds. So long as this
was possible by peaceful methods,- such
methods were employed. I cannot say
that these- methods always redounded to
the credit of the traders, but it is certain
that, whatever . the acts of Individuals,
the central government was so anxious
for peace that it did not criticize too
closely the methods by which it waa
maintained. As the spheres of trade
grew \ larger j and larger, the aboriginal
tribes felt the inconvenience and Injustice
of losing their lands, even at a price, and
attacked the traders. Then followed ex
peditions, mobile, well organized and well
armed, resulting in the death of many
natives and the subjugation of the rest.
The terms enforced, however, were sel
dom hard and frequently the natives who
had fought against the white men were
almost at once' organized as native police
or native regiments to guard the trading
interests.
Examples of Trade Development.
In several instances the fact that the
British policy in colonizing is to develop
trade has received striking proof. Â»I re
fer to "those cases in which charters have
been granted to companies enabling them
to develop territories until such a time
as they were fit to be taken under Brit
ish protection. Such companies from the
time of the Great India Company have
generally succeeded in a marvelous man
ner and have gained for the British many
prosperous colonies and territories. In
recent years there have been the Royal
Niger Company, the North Borneo Com
pany and the British South African Com
pany. The first two have now given place
to British protectorates and eventually
the British South Africa Company, that
enormous creation of Mr. Rhodes, will
follow their example. That this system
of private colonization is good is proved
by results. The German Government
also, in several cases, has introduced it
