The Call, in the interest of the whole livestock in
dustry, raised the voice of warning long ago, and
foretold this most distressing situation, which threat
ens injury" to the stockmen, the transportation com
panies, the domestic consumer and the meat export
trade alike. The remedy is easy. Lease the ranges
and devote the proceeds to irrigation. . .
The Denver" Stockman admits that when the fences
are removed the situation will be unbearable, as it will
take but a short time to permanently destroy the
feed, leaving nothing for sheep or cattle. Sheep men
anticipate a glut in the market because sheep that
cannot be fed must be sold, and. cattle men foresee a
shortage in feed before the end of summer and the
expulsion of their herds from the range by sheep.
On Otter Creek, Wyoming, 2000 sheep have been
killed by driving them over a cliff, and the sheep men
at Sheridan are arming for war.
A citizen of Wyoming admits that the cattle men
have no defense left but their ; Winchesters. He
says: "Personally I am not in favor of that kind of
a fight, but there are plenty of men on the range
who are, and promise to kill every sheep man in the
State as soon as the word is given. The conservative
sheep men and cattle men are doing all they can to
prevent an open conflict," but the situation is growing
worse every day. It is the small cattle-owners and
farmers who are suffering the worst. The sheep mean
ruin for them, and they feel they have the moral
right to protect their range by force. The sheep men
arc fully as desperate. They are all struggling for
enough grass to keep their sheep alive, and they feel
that the public range is as much theirs as the cattle
men's. They are organizing, and while, they are on
the defensive now it will take but little to put them
on the other side of the game. We may stave off
trouble till the fences are all torn down, but after that,
look out for bloodshed."
Near Cheyenne, a cattle man rented a section of land'
and put 120 head of cattle on it. His stock was
driven off in the night by sheep men and so scattered
that he did not Tecover them.
In Gunnison County, Colorado, the sheep got
across the line and the Colorado fighters have killed
several thousand of them, first making prisoners of
the herders.
The railroads have promised not to bring any more
sheep into Colorado from the ranges that have been
fed out by them .in Southern Utah. But tens , of
thousands of sheep have been driven to the Colorado
line, which is patroled by an armed guard to keep
them out.
At Eaton and Lone Tree, Colo., the sheep men
are in a Winchester war with invading sheep men
from Wyoming.
At Pine Bluffs and Grover, in that State, the small
cattle-growers and farmers, finding themselves, likely
to be starved out by sheep, have assaulted three sheep
camps, burned the wagons and driven the sheep off
the range. Â»
In Star Valley, Wyoming, the cattle men are also
land-owners, as cattle men usually are, and the sheep
have fed the range bare to their fences. They have or
ganized in self-defense and sent a warning notice to
the sheep men, with the intention of offering armed
resistance. Â¦ > , ~
The- cattle men in the Pecos Valley, New Mexico,
have been driven but by invading sheep and have re
treated, fighting. One, man has been killed in the
war.
A dispatch from Pierre, S. D., says that sheep are
pouring into, the Black Hills by 'ten's of thousands,
and the best cattle ranges are being occupied by them.
In consequence cattle men see their doom ; and are
selling their herds at a sacrifice or moving them on,
knowing that the range destroying sheep will over
take them wherever they go.
In this manner sheep first drove cattle from the
Oregon range, and now Mr. Mills, livestock agent of
the Oregon Railway and Navigation Company, says
that 2,000,000 sheep. will starve in Oregon this year
unless they are taken- elsewhere to feed. It is pro
posed to send them to the public ranges of Nebraska
and Kansas, where they will repeat the destruction
that has made impossible their maintenance in
Oregon.* -
Heretofore by drift fences and -various devices for
inclosing the arid. ranges on: the public domain the
stockmen have managed to .eontrorand preserve some
feed. But now.it is decided that these fences and in
closures are contrary to law, and the Government is
tearing - them, down and ; those . who used them are,
being criminally indicted. ; The cattle interest is the
principal sufferer, because the moment sheep are
driven on the range cattle leave it, as they will not
feed where sheep run. An actually free public range
means a range monopolized by sheep, and they soon
turn it into a'desert. V
THE Denver Stockman reports cattle receipts at
that point declined to only one-fourth the usual
number, arid attributes the shortage to the lack
of feed caused by destruction of the forage in the wild
scramble for the free range.

Reception to Colonel McBride.
Marc Augenblick, liquor merchant, 1047
Market street, swore to a complaint in
Judge Mogan's court yesterday for the
arrest of Horace L. Poulin on the charge
of misdemeanor embezzlement. Poulin.
was a collector for Augenblick and is ac
cused of embezzling amounts aggregat
ing $45.
Poulin came Into notoriety about eight
een months ago, when Amy Murphy, a
telephone operator, committed suicide in
Golden. Gate Park because he had dis
carded her. The case caused considerable
excitement at the time, and Poulin: was
arrested, but subssequently discharged, as
no offense could be charged against him.
He left the city for New York, and
Augenblick says he took pity on him and
brought him back recently and gave him
employment.

The act of the Legislature, approved April 11,
1S78, gave the Park Commissioners "all the
powers over Point Lobou avenue," as if the
same had "been originally a part of Golden
Gate Park." Section 4 of the act approved
April 4 says "that the board shall have full
and exclusive power to govern, manage and
direct the said j>arks and avenues."
Queryâ Are the parts of the franchises grant
ed November 6. 1878. running along Point Lobos
avenue, from Central to First avenue, and the
franchise of 1890, running along Point Lobos
avenue, from First to Fifth avenue, valid? >
Queryâ Is the franchise of November 6, 1878.
covering exactly the same ground aa the fran
chise of April 13, 1S77, with the additions of the
two blocks from Geary to Stockton street, a
valid one?
The oroiglnal franchise granted the Geary
street Itailroad for twenty-five years from
April 13. 1S77, commenced at Stockton street
and ran along Geary street and Point Lobos
avenue to First avenue, and thence to the park.
The following year another franchise for twen
ty-five years was granted, to expire November
6, 1903, commencing at Kearny street and run
ning along Geary street to Stockton and along
and covering the same streets as .the franchise
of the year previous. The extension of Â¦ the
above was granted from September 17, 1899, to
run across Kearny street to Market. In 1890
a franchise was granted to expire November 6,
1903, along Point Lobos avenue, from First ave
nue to Fifth, and thence to the park.

The ordinance repealing orders requir
ing street railway companies to provide
guarda for cars and dummies for tne pur
pose of preventing accidents and loss of
life and prescribing the character of life
guards or. fenders to be attached to street
railroad cars was finally passed. This al
lows the State law to govern and will
compel the companies to install adequate
safeguards.
Supervisor McCarthy, a member of the
committee, stated that most of the rail
way companies are not operating under
their original franchises, wmch stipulated
the amount of the license tax. McCarthy
said that the report of the committee
would be in favor of an increase.
City Attorney Lane's o'plnion holds that
where no license tax has been fixed in a
franchise granted to a street railway cor
poration It is within the power of the city
to" compel payment of such reasonable
license tax as the legislature of the mu
nicipality may prescribe. Where, how
ever, the franchise fixes the amount of
the license tax which* may be charged and
expressly denies to the municipality
power to increase such amount the board
then has not the power to increase the
license.
The ordinance Increasing the license on
street cars from $15 to $50 per annum was
reref erred to .the Committee on' License
and Orders by the Board of Supervisors
yesterday, and in accordance with an
opinion filed ' by the City Attorney it Is
probable that the bill will be recommend
ed for passage.

Alaska Packers' Association Says
That It Will Accept Asssssor's
Ultimatum.
The Alaska Packers' Association has
signified its intention to no longer resist
the payment of the franchise tax. Word
was conveyed to the Assessor yesterday
That all further legal proceedings would
be discontinued and the amount of the
assessment. $3250. will be paid into the
treasury within a day or two.
At the time Assessor Dodge levied the
tax on the franchises of it large number
at corporation* 5 , considerable opposition
was manifested, many of tne corporations
threatening to withdraw their principal
offices from the Stale. Among these was
the Alaska Packers' Association. A
northern port was to be selected as its
principal place of business and there the
outfitting of ships, gathering of crews
and purchasing cf 6upplies would be un
dertaken. After a. serious consideration
of the difficulties to be encountered and
the undisputed 1 advantages of San Fran
cisco as an outfitting point. It was de
cided to remain here.
"This action on thj part of the Alaska
Packers' Association, said Assessor
Dodge, "presages a change of base on the
part of other corporations which have
been resisting the payment of taxes as
sessed on their franchises. I would esti
mate from the Information that I have
received that taxes on assessed franchise
valuations of $3/>00.000 will be paid Into
the treasury. This â¢will materially in
crease the surplus in the treasury."

Olaf Anderson Grieves Life Away on
Account of the Loss of
His Wife.
Olaf Anderson, a' Norwegian, is slowly
grieving his life away in the house of a
friend, at 721 Minna street on account of
the death of his wife, and three little chil
dren, dependent on his earnings, are des
titute. 'The sad case has been reported to
the Society for the Prevention of Cruelty
to Children and Secretary White has the
matter under investigation.
Anderson came to this country from
Norway several years ago, brinering his
wife with him. He was sober and indus
trious and for some time prospered. His
wife finally became ill and the earnings of
long years of labor were spent in a vain
endeavor to restore her to health. Two
years ago she died, leaving her husband
with four children.
He was heartbroken and lost all inter
est in life. Except when urged to action
by his friends, he would sit all day brood-
Ing over his misfortune. He worked only
at intervals and then mechanically, with
no hope or spirit. Finally he became too
weak to work more and he has since been
cared for by the charity of his fellow
countrymen.
Anderson and his children were taken in
by John Hansen, but the latter is poor
and feels that he can no longer bear the
expense of two families. -Letters of
guardianship have been applied for and
the children will probably be placed in
some charitable institution.

THE McGLADE TRIAL
The Taking of Testimony Will Begin
This Morning' Before Judge
Burnett.
The fourth trial of Peter TV. McGlade
on the charge of forgery was commenced
yesterday morning before Judge A. G.
Burnett of Santa Rosa in Judge Cook's
court. Attorney Lennon represented the
defendant and District Attorney Byington
and Assistant District Attorney Deuprey
represented the people.
There was very little time wasted In se
curing the Jury. . Before the adjournment
at noon nine had been secured and the
other three were chosen during the after
noon. The Jurors are: W. Lowenbesg;
broker; R. E. Darbee, wholesale oyster
merchant; Thatcher Emery, retired cap
italist; L. Van Laak, broom manufactur
er; H. C. McClure, agent: George Kilpat
rick, retired capitalist; Jacob Schwerdt,
furniture dealer; J. P. Eklund. stationer:
Thomas Johnstone, secretary; Arthur
Deittle. merchant; James Gorie, and John
Williams. - â¢ -
After the Jury was secured it was de
cided to continue the case till this morn
ing, when testimony will be heard.
Steals Clothes From Steamers.
Manuel Gestres was booked at the City
Prison by Detective Egan yesterday on
the charge of burglary. Saturday, it Is
alleged, he entered a room on the steamer
Sonoma and stole a suit of clothes, leav
ing his old suit in exchange.
With this board of directors and all
differences settled among the old stock
holders, all of the lawsuits settled and
dismissed, and Mr. Charles Newman, who
was the active factor in pushing forward
the work for the old company, again
swinging Into line to promote the success
of the new corporation, there Is little
doubt that the ship will take flight within
the next ninety days, and all of its stock
holders will reap the reward which they
are Justly entitled to for their persistent
efforts in pioneering and making a suc
cess cf this most impartant branch of
scientific navigation.
Now, however, the whole matter has
been amicably arranged by the sale of the
plant tc a new corporation. . organized for
that purpose, called the "California Aerial
Navigation Company," and the directors
of this company are in themselves a suf
ficient guarantee that the business will
be carried forward in such a manner as
will satisfy all parties concerned.
Mr. I. J. Truman of the Columbian
Banking Company Is president. Mr. Frank
W. Page of the Vulcan Smelting and Re
fining Company is secretary and the other
mrrnbers of the board comprise the fol
lowing gentlemen: Mr. George Graham,
Dr. Born, Mr. E. ll Sneli. Mr. Leo New
man. Mr. Oscar Menchen, Mr. William
LJruckman and Mr. Charles Stanley.
There have been good and sufficient
reasons, however, why this promise was
not kept. There is nothing at fault with
the mechanism or construction of the ship
itself, but financial difficulties Intervened
to delay its progress; the stockholders be
came diFFatisfUid with certain members of
the uoard of directors, and this difficulty
found its way into the courts. When this
happens all efforts to carry on an enter
prise must prove useless, and only time
wi!l serve to overcome the law'6 delays.
The public has been justly disappointed
on account of the. -ar.kept promises of the
Stanley Aerial Navigation Company to
construct its ship and rc&eem its pledges
to navigate the air, which it promised it
v.ould do in ths month of Hay.
All impediments in the way of its suc
cessful navigation are overcome.

The Board of Supervisors yesterday
passed to print an ordinance granting an
extension of ninety days from June 27,
1901, to the Bay and Coast Railway Com
pany in which to expend the sum of $25,
000 in construction work on its proposed
line from this city to Santa Cruz. The
extension was granted because the Board
of Works notified the Supervisors that
the official grades on the proposed route
have not been fixed and the tracks will
cut into the naln outlet of the North
Point sewer. The board will establish
the grades in the meantime. Reed op
posed the extension because the road has
not fifty miles of track, as required by
the charter, and McCarthy also objected
because the company had not agreed to
substitute electricity for steam In the city
limits when deemed necessary by the
board.

. WHEATâ Liverpool was not materially
changed, but Paris was higher, owing to the
unfavorable crop prospects in Russia and
France, and the French markets were quoted
very firm. An Antwerp cable said that In
some sections of Southern Russia the crop is
almost destroyed by heat. A cable from the
Argentine said that sellers were giving way
and that supplies were increasing.
The English visible supply Increased 1,489,
000 bushels. The . world's shipments for the

tine. 16,000 from India and 21,000 from Aus
tralia. â¢ .

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
San Francisco is probably the coolest sta
tion in the United States to-night. Unusually
Â¦warm weather prevails over the country west
of the Rocky Mountains. With the exceptiou
of a narrow strip along the coast, the entire
Western half of the country reports maximum
tpmperatures * exceeding 80. In Southern
Arizona temperatures from 100 to 104 are re
rortcd. In th<? Sacramento and San Joaquin
Valleys the temperatures range from SS to *S.
No rain has fallen west of the Rocky Moun
tains.
There has been but little change in pressure
and conditions are not favorable for settled
fair weather. . .

CHICAGO, June 17. â The improved tone of
Liverpool cables, coupled with a decrease in the
world's shipments of quantity on passage, gave
the wheat market a good start to-day, the July
delivery opening unchanged to %c lower, f On
covering by shorts, who were moved to action
by . reports of k big crop damage in Continental
Europe^ there was an early advance to 70%c,
at which trading was mostly of a local nature,
though there was some buying for export.' Dis
appointment was felt by the bulls over the
c>mall decrease in the visible supply and the
long wheat accumulated on the up-turn was put
out, breaking the price to 69%c. Some consid
erable purchases by the elevator people caused
a reaction to "O'.ic, at which the market closed
hie lower and fairly steady. The visible supply
decreased 162,000 bushels, against 1,640,000 bush
els the week before and 231,000 bushels a year
ago. The world's shipments of wheat and flour
were equal to S, 135, 000 bushels, and the quantity
on ocean pasage decreased 1,136,000 bushels.
The bull movement caused the notable appre
ciation in corn which was on hand at the begin
ning of business to-day, despite the reports of
a continuation of excellent weather in the corn
belt, the July option opening firm between 42%c
and 43%c and closing steady %c down at 42%c.
The oats market was more active than any
other. July closed %Â®Uc improved at 28%c.
The provision . market was dull and bare of
speculative interest. The tone was easier on. a
larger run of hogs than was expected with low
er prices. July pork closed 7%c lower and lard
closed a shade lower, with ribs 21ic down.

CHEYENNE. Wyo., June 17.â Marcus
Boegeli, who until recently conducted a

MASK THALL GOING EAST
. FOR P1LAYS AND PI.AYER3
Mark Thall, the well known theatrical
manager, and Charles Bryant, stage man
ager of. the Alcazar, leave to-night for
New York on business. They will be
away for two or three weeks, and during
their stay In the East will perfect plans
for reproducing a number of late New
York successes at the Alcazar and Central
theaters. Mr. Thall will also engage a
number of new people for the local stock
companies.
Â¦ : As a result of this trip the theater-goers
on this coast will have an opportunity of
seeing a very strong stock company in the
latest and most popular plays. It is the
intention of Messrs. Belasco, Thall and
Mayer to form a chain of theaters In the
big. cities on this coast. It will not be In
the nature of a trust, but simply a com
bination to enlarge the theatrical field
and give certain cities the benefit of their
successful experience in running stock
houses. It will be their -purpose to keep
strong stock companies on the road and
play at the various theaters under their
control.
During Mr. Thall's absence In the East
Fred Belasco and Maurice E. Mayer will
look after the two local show houses.
Among many leading Republicans who>
actively participated in the prolonged Sen
atorial contest at Sacramento and made
a gallant and successful fight to maintain
the honor of the State there are several
who seem determined to ascertain,
whether our senior Senator, George C.
Perkins, proposes to recognize their serv
ices as valuable to the party. Last Fri
day State Senators Belshaw, Caldwell and
Smith ha< a heart-to-heart talk with the
United Staft* Senator who dwells In Ala
meda County and expects that county to
send to the Legislature a delegation,
pledged to use all honorable means to
bring about his re-election to the United
States Senate." Plain language was used
at the conference. The State Senators at
the conclusion of the interview expressed
the opinion that Senator Perkins would
respect the wishes of Republicans who
haa opposed boss rule in party affairs.
It was readily perceived that the Sen
ator would, prefer to go right along and
make the contest for his re-election on the>
basis of his own personal fitness and- past
service without touching the principles of
party Integrity which were recognizei In
.the. battle which culminated in the elec
tion of Senator Bard, but the visiting del
egation contended that the Senator should
come fairly into line with the anti-boss
forces of the State and assist In upholding
the principles of good government and
clean politics. . .
There was no demand that Republicans
who opposed the election of Senator Bard
should be turned down and Ignored, but
there was a demand that Federal office
holders who went to Sacramento and.
made themselves offensively conspicuous
in the advocacy of boss methods of manip
ulating the Legislature should not be re
appolnted to office.
As a general proposition Senator Per
kins is in favor of reappointlng or retain
ing in service for an Indefinite length of
time the present regime of Federal office
holders. Frank Leach's four years' term
as Superintendent of the Mint expired last
week. W. W. Montague's four years'
term as Postmaster of San Francisco will
expire next week. There are no indica
tions and no demand for a change In
either office. John P. Irish, Naval Officer,
and Joseph S. Spear, Surveyor of Cus
toms, will probably serve throughout
President McKinley's administration. The
term for which United States Marshal
Shine was commissioned will not expire
until next year. John C. Lynch'a term as
Collector of Internal Revenue will expire
within two months. The offices of Col
lector of the Port and United States At
torney for the Northern District of Cali
fornia were recently filled by appoint
ment. J. M. Gleaves, United States Sur
veyor General, and Charles Bassett.
United States* Shipping Commissioner, are
willing to keep right along In the service.

SARANAC LA.KE, N. Y.,- June 17.âSo
cial and religious circles nere were elec
trified yesterday morning directly after
church, when the news, got abroad that
Rev. William Tatlock, pastor of the Pres
byterian church, was to be challenged to
a duel to the death by Harry Gilmore,
son of Colonel Gilmore of v the regular
army. The rumor was immediately veri
fied by young Gilmore, who, instead of
going to church, spent the morning hurry
ing around Saranac in search j of friends
to act as seconds. A friend of Tatlock
Bald late last night:- '.
Â¦ 'T.' hear from Gilmore's headquarters
that he is now writing out a formal, chal
lenge. How Tatlock will treat it I don't
know. I believe,) however, that the pastor
has already written an account of the en
tire matter to Gilmore's mother, and it
is quite likely that if neither of the com
batants Is killed In the contest one, at
least, of them will get spanked. If, as we
all hope, Tatlock accepted Gilmore's chal
lenge, I know at least two of his elders
who will act : as seconds on the field."
; Injthe meantime all Saranac -1st torn up
.over the prospect of. such a sensational
event as a real duel. Mr, Gilmore is pre
paring for a West Point examination and
Tatlock has served him. In the- capacity
of tutor. The misunderstanding is the re
sult of a disagreement over a- question In
mathematics., . .â¢ ; \ â¢ .-. .
Son ,of Colonel Gilmore
K Out for His Tutor's-
Life-Blood.
The third lecture of the series -which is
being delivered by Dr. Benjamin de Costa
drew a large audience' last" night : to
Metropolitan Temple. In treating his sub
ject, which was " "The Bible Among the
Masses of the American -People," the
eloquent speaker argued that the Catholic
Church is the only true exponent of the
Bible, as the infallible word of God, and
Â¦that the church had always interpreted
it in the light of progress and* .true
science. He vigorously defended the
teachings of the holy book against the at
tacks of the high critics. ...
Rev. Father Peter C. Yorke presided
and in his introductory remarks p,aicl' a
high compliment to the ability and worth
of Dr. de Costa, saying that there are
none more able than he to . defend tho
Bible. At the conclusion of Dr. de Costa' s
address, Father Yorke took occasion to
make a few remarks,. ._ in which he
vehemently criticized the modern press,
stating that in. their columns might be
seen side by side reports of sermons and
sensational stories.. He, argued that the
Â¦^papers should -keep outr-of the pulpit and
the sanctuary^-': ; ,; \ â¢ Â¦â¢â¢- i
Dr. dfe Costa- spoke^ in part -as follows:
' .It has been declared .by some of 'the modern
scholars that no person .'of. education accepts
the teachings of. the Bible as Infallible. ; Then
Leo XIII is uneducated; then all .the graduates
pf the colleges and . seminaries, and all the
clergy of the world are. uneducated. 1 ; v ; i.
Catholic -Church Can Defend Bible.
. The non-Catholics can . make no defense of
their doctrines because : their beliefs are. not
definitely outlined. They, cannot defend the
Bible, because they know- not what part of it
to defend. . On the other hand . the Catholic
Church can defend the entire Bible as the in
fallible word of. God. For a long time before
I became a convert I saw in the distance the
strong walls of the Catholic Church and deemed
'them impregnable. The non-Catholics have de
fended the Bible until they have destroyed it;
juntil they have driven 50,000,000 people in this
country to disbelieve in its teachings.
\ The Catholic Church has always interpreted
;the Bible in accordance with progress and the
spirit of the age. It wages no conquest be
tween religion and true science.' Around the
Bible the church has built. a wall more Im
pregnable ' than . the Emperors of Rome ever
built around the Imperial City. If we allow a
single doubt to be cast upon any of the teach
ings of the Bible, the whole book Is swept
away. The church is its natural guardian. It
haa been said that the Bible, next to the sac
raments, is most sacred. . Then . as a matter
of right the church should be its guardian, as
it is the administrator of the sacraments.
Need of an Instructor.
. The Bible does not teach infallible truths to
all. In the use of the Bible we need an in
structor, just as we need an instructor in the
use of a medicine chest. No one of you would
po into a pharmacy and. attempt to order drugs

Actor and Actress Wed.
CHICAGO, June 17.â Miss May Hosmer,
the actress, and George R. Fiske, actor
and singer, were married here to-day.
ment to-night before Notary W. J. Morti
mer, saying:
"This evening Miss Adams came to
Thomas S. Allen's house at 2101 Hearst
avenue, where I reside, and requested
! me to go with her to her home to pro
tect her from her father's abuse. I en
tered the. house with her and walked Into
-a bedroom, where I was met by her father,
Charles Adams. I did not threaten him
or show him any violence. I simply told
him that he must not abuse, his daugh
ter, whereupon Charles Adams shot me.
I then jumped on him and smashed his
! face and beat the devil out of him."
Miss Adams is 22 years of age. She and
her brother Carl, 10 years old, were cared
for at the White residence. Miss Vera
White said:
"Miss Adams was in constant fear of
her father. He has been crazy drunk most
of the time-since they have been here
and has had delirium tremens. He beat
Miss Adams, and she was afraid that he
would kill her. She is prostrated and In a
very low condition."
At the Receiving Hospital Adams made
the following statement:
Claims He Was Justified.
"Jessup and I are the best of friends,
but he interfered in my business and I
shot him. I told him I would shoot him.
My daughter let him into the house and
I heard him coming up the stairs. I was
in my room at the time. I took my pistol
and went to the head of the stairs and
warned Jessup not to come up. I told
him I was irresponsible, that I was in an
angry mood and that I would shoot, .but
he kept right on coming. Then to avoid
trouble I backed into my room, though
still pointing the pistol at him.
"When he got to the threshold of my
door I thought it was time to act, so I
fired one shot. I kept telling him not to
come, but he did not heed me. I had no
intention of killing him. He was the last
man In the world I would have killed.
That was why I shbt him where I did. I
aimed for his left shoulder. I fired only
one shot. I had five others that I could
have fired, but ray object was only to stop
him. The revolver is a Smith & Wesson
that I've had years.
"As soon as I fired Jessup ran toward
me and grappled me around the body. I
didn't make much of a resistance. Jes
sup gave me a terrible beating with the
butt end of my own pistol. I don't blame
him a bit. I hope he recovers and I'm
sorry that I had to shoot him, but when
a man interferes with another man that's
what he gets. . ' â¢Â¦
Praises Man He Shot.
"Jessup and 1 are old friends. He is
a perfect gentleman. I don't think he
ever did a wrong thing In his life. But
he uid interfere with me, and interference
in my affairs I will not brook. There was
one thing that I resented, and that was
Jessup's attentions to my daughter. He
is a married man with a daughter of his
own, though his wife is separated from
him. But then that was not why I shot
Jtssup. I would not have shot him . on
that account, because I consider him per
fectly honorable. There was nothing in
the doctor's action to create suspicion
and nothing in my daughter's actions
that were reprehensible. I will stand by
my daughter's charactetr, and I will
stand by Jessup's character.
"I'm all right, but I hate to be dis
turbed when I'm drunk. I've been drunk
almost right along now for two years. I
left the ministry because I couldn't- get
drunk when I wanted to, and I've been
drinking ever since."

THE DISASTER
Railroad Officials Inves
% tigate the Wreck
at Napa. V,'-
NAP A, June 17.â There Is still much
mystery about the cause of the disaster
to the Napa Valley train .near the sta
tion here last evening, and all the efforts'
of railroad detectives have not been re
warded with anything, like an adequate
explanation. The opinion of the railroad
men who work here that some person 'or
persons deliberately sought to destroy the
train has not suffered any change by rea
son of the developments to-day. It has
been suggested that the changing of the
switch may be ascribed to the careless
ness of some railway employe, but the
majority of people who have investigated
the affair refuse to accept this theory. ,
Special Agent Meloney of the Southern
Pacific Company arrived here early to
day and at once began an Inquiry: as to
the circumstances attending the disaster.
He spent several hours in questioning em
ployes and in an inspection of the track.
The results of. his inspection were not
made known, and he went back to San
Francisco on an afternoon train. . .
It was reported to-day that several per
sons whose conduct at the time seemed
suspicious were seen near the switch
south of the station a short time before
the train from Valle jo arrived- and 'ran
into the freight cars. The story *was only
partially verified, however, and Constable
George Secord, who had charge of Â¦' this
part of -the investigation, is inclined to
doubt the truth of it. ; " Â¦
It Is the opinion of the constable that
the- negligence of a railroad - employe
caused, the wreck, but he can assign no
reason r why three trains ' : should have
passed safely before the. disaster if the
switch was out of order. The lock was
found closed after the wreck, ' and this
fact would indicate that a person owning
a key- and familiar with the mechanism
tampered with it at some time in. the af
ternoon. -' '
Engineer '.Rutherford, whose ankle was
sprained, is at his , home ; in Â¦ Calistoga.
Mrs. Burgess, one, of the passengers, who
is believed to be suffering . from' slight
concussion of the brain, is. in San Fran-.
Cisco. When the crash came she < was
thrown â¢ out of her ; seat and struck her
head' on an iron bar That surrounds the
car stove. Dr. Pulslfer, who attended
her. said to-night that she Is . not dan
gerously injured. Â»
Mrs. Frederick. Blanchard, another
woman, who â¢, was hurt,' is Â¦ at her home
here. She was returning from . Berkeley.
The shock of the collision threw her from
her . seat and cne of her shoulders was
.severely bruised. , \
MINISTER MAY
BE MURDERER
John Or. Jessup at
Berkeley.
C. G. Adams Shoots Dr.
Former Episcopal Priest Uses
Pistol in a Fit of Drunken *
Frenzy.
Dentist Attempts to Prevent Father
From Abusing His Daughter and
Receives a Probably Fatal
Wound.
BERKELEY. June 17.-Charles G.
Adams, an unfrocked Episcopal clergy
man, shot and probably fatally wounued
Dr. John G. Jessup, a Berkeley dentist,
at 7 o Â¥ clock this evening. The shooting
occurred at the Adams residence. 1801
Walnut street, where the dentist had gone
to protect Miss Agnes S. Adams from
abuse and beating by her father. Sum
moned to the Adams home by the young
woman, who had fled to him for succor.
the dentist returned with the girl and
started into the bedroom on the upper
floor.
Adams, armed with a revolver and half
crazed from liquor, met the doctor on the
landing and ordered him back. Jessup
expostulated with the parent, who sud
denly raised the pistol and fired. The bul
let plowed through Jessup'6 right lung.
The doctor, staggered by the impact.
Fturnbled back, but recovered himself
quickly. He leaped upon Adams, threw
him down and wrenched the smoking
weapon from his hand. Then to subdue
his intoxicated assailant Jessup ham
mered blow after blow with the butt o*
the pistol upon the ex-preacher's head.
Weakened by the effort and the hem
orrhage from his wound. Jessup staggered
out of the room and downstairs, and fell
fainting upon the front porch, where he
was picked up by John Armstrong, one
of a crowd of neighbors who were at
tracted by the pistol shot. .
Adams Surrenders Himself.
Jessup Makes a Statement.
Miss Adams ran out of the house after
the shooting and sought shelter at Mrs.
Charles White's residence, next door.
Adams locked himself in the bedroom and
remained there for fifteen minutes, until
Town Marshal Charles Kerns arrived.
Then in response to the demand of the
l^ace officer Adams surrendered himself.
He was covered with blood from the
wounds upon his face and head and was
taken at once to the Receiving Hospital
in Oakland, where his injuries were
dressed. His Ho was split open and his
scalp was cut and torn in several places.
Adams was iiitoxicated when he was
taker, to the hospital and his story was
rambling- and incoherent, principally -con
lined to denials of abuse of his daughter.
Dr. Jessup was removed after the shoot-
Ing to his rooms in the residence of
Thomas S. Alien at 2101 Hearst avenue.
Dr. O. B. Hoagland and Dr. J. S. East
man attended the wounded man and
found that the bullet had entered the left
lung above the heart and had lodged under
the left shoulder blade. The wound is an
extremely dangerous one and the chances
arf against Jessup' s recovery.
The shooting to-night is the culmination
cf a long period of abusive conduct of the
former cleric toward his daughter, who
had often told her friends that she feared
her father when In his cups would kill
her. Frequently she had gone to Jessup
Tor protection when her father, 'in his
drunken ugliness, became dangerously
violent. In his whisky-crazed condition
to-night he threatened the girl, and she
ran out to find a protector. Such is her
etcry.

LOS GATOS, June 17.â Three thousand
excursionists attended the picnic held here
tÂ«--day under the auspices of the Bunker
Hill Association, the Sons of the American
Revolution, the California Association of
Pioneers and the St, Albans Literary and
Social Society.
i The excursionists arrived on two sec
tions of a broad-guage train at 11:30
o'clock and proceeded to Bunker Hill
Park. Here literary exercises were held.
The opening number was a medley of pa
triotic airs rendered by the Second Regi
ment Band. A prayer was offered by
Rev. T. J. Lacey, the chaplain of the day.
After a few oDening remarks by Hon.
William G. Badger and vocal muBic, con
sisting of "The Star Spangled Banner" by
Wiss Juliet Greninger and "The Sword of
Bunker Hill" by O. Lafayette Lienau, the
; oration of the day was delivered bv Col
; onel E. F. Preston. The sneaker ex
jpatiated on the event that the celebration
â¢Â¦ commemorated and traced the progress of
! the nation from that time to the present.
The address was a masterly one and
elicited liberal applause from the audi
ence. It was followed by a song, "Red,
White and Blue," by Emil Barrangon and
"The Marseillaise" by Miss Grace I. Sav
age. The audience then sang "America"
; aria the exercises were closed by a bene
; diction by the chaplain.
i Most of the excursionists brought lunch
I with them and tables were spread under
j neath the oak trees of the park. Those
I vho did not come prepared had their
| wants well supplied by the local hotels
and the ladies of the Presbyterian church,
who took advantage of the occasion to
swell their organ fund by a lunch. After
j lunch the athletic contests were called.
The contests were managed by a com
mittee consisting of Robert Collier, J. S.
Miller and "W. B. Kohn. The prizes were
donated by San Francisco merchants and
were awarded to the successful contest
ants by Lee D. Windrem. Phil J. Euler
and Hugh Clark, who acted as judges.
This is the third time these patriotic so
cieties have met in Los Gatos, the first
occasion being three years ago, when the
park was dedicated and given the name
of Bunker Hill Park.
Quiet Observance in City.
Californlans did not participate in the
gallant defense of Bunker Hill on June
17, 1775, but in respect to the valor of
New England men who fought so gallant
ly against the British on that memorable
occasion every recurring anniversary of
the event is fitly observed in San Fran
cisco by a general display of American
flags. Yesterday the starry banner was
flung to the breeze from many a building
in the city. In promoting the spirit of
national pride in the achievement at
Bunker Hill the association deriving its
name from the battle-field has performed
patriotic service in California^

tingly Commemorate
the Day.

LONDON, June '17.â Replying to ques
tions, in -the House of Commons to-day,
Mr. Brodrick, the' War Secretary, said
there were 40,229 persons in the concen
tration camps of the Transvaal and Or
ange River, Colony. â¢ The deaths in these
camps for the month of May . numbered
98 men and women and 31S children. The
announcement of .the mortality was re
ceived with groans from â¢ the Irish mem
i.bers and cries of "scandalous!" Â» . . :
Mr. Brodrick.- added that the authori
ties were arranging for the release .of the
women and children who had friends to
receive them, but the Government 'could
not undertake to feed " them, in Isolated
places. . â¢ â¢ Â¦ . ' 'â¢ . - Â¦
The division- in the House, of Commons
on the motion made by Mr. Lloyd-George,
Welsh Nationalist, to adjourn the House
on the xjuestion of the treatment of Boer
women and children, which was rejected
by a voteof 253 to X3-C served to accentu- ;

ADVERTISEMENTâ Office of the Chief En-
gineer, Division of the Philippines, Manila,
P. I.. March 1. 1901â Sealed proposals . for
constructing a harbor at Manila, P. I., will
be received at this office until 11- o'clock a.
m., August 1, 1901, and then publicly opened.
Work to be done includes about 150,000 cubic
yards riprap, 21,000 cubic yards concrete and
rubble masonry In breakwaters, about 5.000,-
000 cubic yards of dredging and a pile bulk-
head 4700 feet long. Dredging in mud, sand
and shells to a depth of thirty feet. Dredged
materials to be used for reclaiming land.
Bids must be accompanied by a bond of one
hundred thousand dollars ($100,000) in gold.'
One contract will be made for the whole
work. Contractor's bond will be 10 per cent
of the amount bid. The right is reserved to
reject any or all bids. For specifications and
blank forms apply to this office. Bureau of
Insular AfTairs, Washington. D. C. ; Unite!
States Engineer Offices at New York, Chi-
cago and San Francisco. JOHN -BIDDLE,
Captain. Engineers. : <

W. H. Mills Enthusiastic
Over California's Fine
W. H. Mills, who has charge of the Cali
fornia exhibit at the Pan-American Expo
sition, has returned home. fora stay of a
month or more. He is enthusiastic over
the splendid showing that California has
made and says that it is the finest State
exhibit at the exposition, and that great
results are sure to come of it.
"The exhibitions from California," he
.said/ "are. not alone restricted to the in
dustries but:we also exploit our possibili
ties. For instance, we show fruit, grain
and forestry, but we also have on exhibi
tion samples . of silk and pineapples to
show visitors what California is capable
of doing. - .
"The California, exhibition is, the talk
of the fair and. the attendants of the
California department are busy: all tho
time answering questions . and giving in
formation." The attendance at the Pan-
American Exposition is- good now, but in
the fall, when the harvesting is over, the
numbers will increase 'enormously.' The

six holes, match play, on successive days. En

Slight .differences between the late strik
ing - butchers who returned to work and
their employers caused a temporary riffle
of excitement yesterday at the Turk
street Temple, where headquarters of the
Journeymen Butchers' Union were main
tained. Nothing serious developed, and it
was reported that the majority of the
strikers had been employed again. Ex
ceptions were made in some instances, but
these were entirely of an individual char
acter. Everything was finally made har
monious, and those who were most active
in promoting the strike were told to "call
again."
At the headquarters of the ironworkers
Secretary Wisler reported that two shops
nad been unionized during the day and
about forty-nve ironworkers had resumed
their old positions. As to any disaffection
in the ranks of the strikers, he said that
reports to that effect had been so often
circulated that he was tired of denying
them. Â¦ About fifty men not connected,
with the local organizations of iron
workers went to worK yesterday and more
are expected to take the place of the
striking machinists to-day in various
shops.
"In the two shops orgunized to-day,"
'said Secretary Wisler, "the full demands
of the union have been granted and we
expect further accessions. The men are
slowly_ gaining all their demands."
The ~ executive committee of Beer
Drivers' Union No. 27 adopted a provis
ional series of by-laws to be submitted to
the various branches throughout the
State. -An election i committee was ap
pointed to supervise - the general election
of officers, with a view of forming a cen
tral council of the organization. Nom
inations will be made at the next meeting
and closed at the first meeting in July.
The annual contract to be submitted to
the employing bottlers, the same as the
present year, was presented and adopted.
This must be ratified before July 15.
Metal Polishers Growing Healthy.
.At the meeting of the Metal Polishers'
Association at 1133 Mission street the out
standing members reported . that j they
were gaining in health and strength every
day, and the longer they were out of
work the better it would suit them.
* The following statement has been issued
by the Retail Trades Council:
The executive committee of the Retail Trades
Council held Its regular weekly meeting at the
headquarters of the Cooks' and Walters'
Union, with President A. R. Andre in the
chair.
Business Agent Lane of the Cooks and
Waiters reported several more restaurants
have taken union cards and union crews, and
the number of union window cards now in use
is rapidly approaching the four hundred mark.
Hotels are also rapidly unionizing:.
Business Agent Wllle of the Bakers' Union
reported that employers are using- . desperate
measures to get the bakers to go back to
work, but without success. Shares in the co
operative bakery are selling- rapidly, and in a
week the Advance Co-operative Bakery will be
in a position to set many of the union mem
bers to work who are now on strike. -
Business Agent Sganzinl reported that the
Italian bakers are working- a six-day week,
with the exception of about seven employes of
three non-union concerns.
Information received from Oakland showed
that the Cooks' and Walters' Union there Is
In a snlendid condition. The six-day week is
won both for cooks and waiters, the union
cards are displayed in the windows and the
union has assessed its members 50 cents per
week to .assist the . cocis and waiters of San
Francisco.
In view of the fact that the treasury of the
Cooks' and "Walters* Union was in a better
condition than one week agro, it was resolved
to increase the force of pickets In front of the
unfair restaurants.
The cooks and waiters now regard the strike
as virtually . won, only a few non-union res
taurants being left to be unionized. Members
are paying their assessments willingly and
support Is coming in from the East. A break
in the - ranks of the Restaurant Keepers' Asso
ciation Is ' expected at any time, as the boy
cott is bsKlnnlng to be effective, public opin
ion being now wholly in favor of the cooks and
waiters. J. C. LANE, Secretary

BARBERS MEET
NEW OFFICIALS
With Few Exceptions
Butchers Return to
Work.
F. G... Coburn was brought to the City
Prison yesterday afternoon bySecret Ser
vice Agent Hazen and Operator Moffitt
and locked .up in the tanks pending his
surrender to the United States Marshal
to-day.
Coburn came to this city from Manila
on the transport Kilpatrick last February
and called at the United States secret
service office in quest of employment, he
representing himself as a secret service
agent of the Government in Manila and
Honolulu. He showed a letter of recom
mendation purporting to have been signed
by Alfred Wilkie, chief of the United
States Secret Service Â¦ Bureau at Wash
ington, recommending Coburn as a first
class detective and stating that Coburn
had been in the service of the Govern
ment for twelve.years. He also exhibited
a letter from LieutenaTit Trowbridge at
Manila certifying that Coburn had acted
as a duly authorized sercret service agent
there. Another letter purported to be
from Jules Uri- at Honolulu and certified
that Coburn had been in charge of the
bubonic plague quarantine for the Gov
ernment. .
Chief Hazen put himself in communica
tion with Chief "Wilkie and was informed
that Coburn was a faker and that the
Wilkie letter was a forgery. Yesterday
Coburn called at Hazen's office again and
was detained, by Operator Harry Moffitt
until Mr. Hazen appeared and arrested
him, Coburn admitted that the Wilkie
letter was' forged, but said that a friend
of his had signed Wilkie's name. He ran
a board bill in Woodland, Yolo County,
lately, representing himself as a Govern
ment official. Coburn will be charged
with impersonating a , United States of
ficer. . â¢

Asks the Chief*
for a Job.
Argus Bail Bond Question.
The action instituted to determine
whether demands for bail moneys on de
posit in the treasury "must be audited by
the Auditor to secure their payment by
the Treasurer was on argument in Judge
Seawell's court yesterday. Judge Seawell
took the case under advisement.
